THE 
UNITED 
REPUBLIC 
OF 
TANZANIA 
Borrower: 
Amount: 
Terms: 
Description: 
POVERTY 
REDUCTION 
SUPPORT 
CREDIT 
AND 
GRANT 
Financing 
and 
Program 
Summary 
The 
United 
Republic 
of 
Tanzania 
US$132 
million 
($100 
million 
IDA 
Credit 
and 
$32 
million 
IDA 
Grant) 
Standard 
IDA 
terms: 
40-year 
maturity 
with 
a 1 
0-year grace 
period 
The 
proposed 
operation 
of 
US$132 
million 
is 
a 
Poverty Reduction 
Support 
Credit 
(PRSC) 
that 
would 
build 
on 
recent 
reforms 
undertaken 
by 
the 
United 
Republic 
of 
Tanzania 
and 
support 
the 
implementation 
of 
Tanzania’s 
Poverty Reduction 
Strategy 
(PRS) 
as 
summarized 
in 
the 
Poverty  Reduction 
Strategy  Paper 
(PRSP) 
and 
subsequent 
updates 
undertaken 
with 
annual progress 
reports. 
The 
first 
PRSC 
would 
focus 
on 
private 
sector 
development 
and 
public 
sector  management 
to 
complete 
and 
deepen 
the 
reform 
agenda 
defined 
under 
the 
Programmatic Structural 
Adjustment 
Credit 
(PSAC) 
and 
lay 
the 
ground 
for 
attacking 
income 
poverty 
more 
aggressively. 
It 
is 
envisaged 
that 
there 
will 
be 
at  least 
two 
further 
single-tranche  PRSCs 
(each 
up 
to 
US$lSO 
million 
equivalent) 
in 
FY04 
and 
FY05. 
The 
operation 
will 
support 
the 
implementation 
of 
Tanzania’s 
Poverty 
Reduction 
Strategy 
by: 
(i) 
Accelerating growth: 
After 
nearly 
a decade 
of 
macro 
stabilization, 
structural 
reform 
and 
liberalization, 
the 
rate 
of 
growth 
of 
the 
Tanzanian 
economy 
remains 
low 
relative 
to 
the 
requirements 
for 
effective 
poverty 
reduction 
and 
the 
achievement 
of 
the 
Millennium 
Development 
Goals. 
A 
key 
target therefore 
is 
to 
raise 
growth to 
between 
6 
and 
7 
percent 
in 
the 
medium 
term 
by 
promoting 
higher private 
investment, 
reducing 
the 
cost 
of 
doing 
business, 
and enhancing 
the 
efficiency 
of 
the 
Tanzanian 
economy. 
(ii) 
Broadening 
the 
impact 
of 
growth: 
About 
half 
of 
Tanzania’s 
output 
derives 
from 
the 
rural 
economy 
and 
more than 
70 
percent 
of 
its 
population 
depends 
on 
it 
for 
its 
livelihood. 
Most 
of 
the 
reforms 
to 
date 
have 
had 
a 
stronger 
impact 
in 
the 
urban 
and 
service 
sectors. 
Hence 
the 
program 
of 
PRSCs 
will 
support 
efforts 
to 
spread 
the 
impact 
of 
both 
reforms 
and 
resultant 
growth 
to 
the 
rural 
income 
poor 
through: 
Enhancement 
of 
the 
profitability 
of 
agriculture broadly 
and 
export 
products 
of 
rural  origin 
in 
particular,  through 
better 
quality, 
higher 
productivity, 
reduced 
marketing 
cost 
and 
improved 
access 
to 
markets. 
0 
1 
Page  8
Benefits: 
Risk: 
Removal 
of 
remaining 
institutional 
constraints 
on 
rural 
activities; 
0 
Correcting 
the 
disincentives 
arising 
from 
the 
overall  tax 
regime, 
specially 
those 
resulting 
from 
the 
overbearing/multiplicity 
of 
local 
taxes 
affecting 
income 
earning 
activities 
in 
rural 
areas. 
(iii) 
Aligning  public 
investment 
with 
PRS 
goals: 
In 
addition 
to 
continued 
efforts 
at 
improving 
the 
private 
investment 
climate, 
there 
is 
now 
a 
need 
to 
bring 
the 
development 
budget 
of 
the government 
in 
line 
with 
the 
strategic 
public 
investment 
requirements 
to 
meet 
the  PRS 
targets  and  support 
the 
growth 
of 
private 
sector 
contribution 
to 
the 
economy and 
livelihoods. 
(iv) 
Increasing 
efficiency 
of 
public 
service 
delivery: 
Building 
on 
processes 
that have 
been 
put 
in 
place 
to 
enhance 
the 
strategic 
allocation 
and 
impact 
of 
public 
resources 
for 
poverty 
reduction, 
increase 
the 
efficiency 
of 
public 
resource 
use 
through 
measures 
to 
further 
strengthen 
accountability 
mechanisms 
combined 
with 
capacity 
building 
at 
the 
local 
and 
central 
government 
level. 
The 
proposed 
series 
of 
PRSCs 
will 
help 
the 
United 
Republic 
of 
Tanzania 
to 
implement 
the 
PRS 
and 
reach 
the 
goals 
set 
out 
in 
the 
strategy. 
The 
reforms 
supported 
by 
the 
operation 
will 
help 
to 
raise 
growth, 
make 
progress 
towards 
achieving 
the 
Millennium 
Development 
Goals  and 
strengthen 
the 
institutional  infrastructure 
needed 
to 
improve 
the 
public 
sector’s 
capacity 
to 
carry 
out 
its 
role 
more  effectively, 
as 
envisioned 
in 
the 
PRS. 
Improved 
delivery 
of 
public 
services 
is 
essential 
if 
the 
United 
Republic 
of 
Tanzania 
is 
to 
reach 
its 
goals 
for 
poverty 
reduction 
and 
improve 
the 
quality 
of 
life 
in 
Tanzania. 
Several 
risks 
pertain 
to 
the 
reform 
program 
supported 
under 
PRSC 
1 
: 
0 
As 
the 
next 
round 
of 
elections 
approaches, 
concerns 
with 
the 
durability 
of 
commitment 
to 
reforms 
will 
mount. 
To 
mitigate 
this 
risk, 
it 
is 
necessary 
to 
lock 
in 
reforms 
undertaken 
by 
the 
United 
Republic 
of 
Tanzania through 
legal 
and 
institutional 
changes 
to 
root 
these 
reforms 
deeply 
both 
in 
attitude 
and 
practice. 
As 
policy-making 
has become 
more inclusive 
with 
a 
greater 
role 
for 
stakeholders 
beyond 
the 
executive, 
in 
particular 
parliament, 
decision-making 
processes 
have 
tended 
to 
become 
more 
drawn 
out. 
This 
poses 
primarily 
a 
risk 
with 
respect 
to 
the 
speed 
of 
implementation 
of 
agreed actions, 
while 
it 
reduces 
the 
risk 
of 
policy 
reversals. 
Another 
risk 
for 
the achievement 
of 
PRS 
objectives 
arises 
from 
the 
ongoing 
decentralization 
process, 
which 
shifts 
responsibility 
for 
key 
poverty reducing 
programs 
in 
education,  health, 
the 
water 
sector, 
and 
agriculture  to 
the 
districts. 
While 
systems 
and 
capacities 
at 
the 
central 
level 
are 
generally 
stronger 
than 
at 
the 
0 
0 
*. 
11 
Page  9
local 
level, 
limited 
human 
resource 
capacities 
to 
implement 
and 
manage 
the 
PRSC 
reform 
agenda 
represent 
another 
risk, especially 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
public 
expenditure 
management, 
procurement, 
and 
financial 
management. 
To 
ensure 
that 
such 
risks 
do 
not 
derail 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
PRS 
and 
PRSC, 
appropriate 
monitoring 
instruments 
have 
been 
put 
in 
place. 
Tanzania’s  annual 
PRS 
progress 
report, 
which builds 
on 
a 
recently 
developed comprehensive 
poverty 
monitoring 
system, 
and 
a 
well-established 
annual 
public

review 
mechanism 
provide 
open 
and 
transparent 
arrangements 
for 
monitoring 
outcomes 
and 
fiduciary 
risks. 
Moreover, 
the

of

provides

for

identified 
in

and

and

on 
necessary

as 
necessary.

I. 
INTRODUCTION 
1. 
This 
program 
document 
presents 
a single-tranche 
Poverty Reduction 
Support 
Credit 
and 
Grant 
(PRSC1) 
to 
the 
United 
Republic 
of 
Tanzania 
in 
the 
amount 
of 
SDR 
96.4 
million 
(US$l32million 
equivalent). 
The 
proposed 
operation selectively 
supports 
the 
implementation 
of 
key 
priority 
areas 
of 
Tanzania’s 
poverty reduction 
strategy 
(PRS), 
as 
set 
out 
in 
the 
Poverty 
Reduction 
Strategy Paper 
(PRSP) 
prepared 
in 
2000 
and 
its 
subsequent 
updates 
in 
the 
first 
and 
second 
PRS 
progress 
reports. 
PRSCl 
is 
the 
first 
of 
a 
series 
of 
three annual PRSC operations. 
A 
key 
feature 
of 
the 
Tanzania 
PRSC 
framework 
is 
a 
focus 
on 
cross-cutting 
and 
institutional 
issues. 
Reforms 
in 
Tanzania 
have 
run 
ahead 
of 
the 
required 
changes 
in 
legal 
and 
institutional 
frameworks. 
To 
reduce 
risks 
of 
reversal, 
the 
PRSC 
program 
places 
particular 
emphasis 
on 
locking 
in 
gains made 
to 
date 
through 
bringing 
both 
the 
legal 
and 
the 
institutional 
frameworks 
in 
line 
with 
the 
achievements 
in 
reforms. 
Sector-specific 
issues 
will 
continue 
to 
be 
dealt 
with 
in 
the 
context 
of 
sector-wide 
programs.  Section 
1V.C 
of 
the 
document 
provides 
details 
about 
complementary 
sector 
programs 
supported 
by 
development 
partners 
including 
the 
World 
Bank. 
The PRSC 
framework 
places a 
strong 
emphasis 
on 
monitoring 
results 
from 
sector 
programs. 
Hence,  a 
number 
of 
agreed  actions  focus 
on 
strengthening 
the 
capacity  and 
institutional 
framework 
for 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
(M&E). 
2. 
Over 
the 
past 
few 
years, 
the 
PRS 
has 
become 
the 
overarching framework 
for policymaking 
in 
and 
donor 
support 
to 
Tanzania. 
The 
second 
annual 
PRS progress 
report 
documents progress 
in 
fine-tuning 
and 
implementing 
the 
PRS 
with 
a 
clearly identifiable impact 
on 
poverty 
and 
social 
indicators 
in 
some 
areas. 
However, 
based 
on 
the 
results 
of 
the 
recently 
completed  2000/01 
household 
budget 
survey 
(HBS), 
the 
progress 
report 
also documents 
deeply 
entrenched 
income 
poverty affecting 
about 
35 
percent 
of 
all 
Tanzanians 
and 
continued 
severe 
deprivation 
as 
reflected 
by 
a 
variety 
of 
poverty 
and 
social 
indicators. 
The report 
has 
also 
shed 
new 
light 
on 
regional 
variations 
in 
poverty 
within 
Tanzania that 
have 
important 
implications for 
the 
PRS. 
Preliminary 
poverty 
and social 
impact 
assessments 
have been 
carried out 
for 
the 
Tanzania 
Poverty 
and 
Human 
Development Report 
(PEIDR) 
by 
the 
Research 
and 
Analysis 
Working 
Group 
under 
the 
Tanzania 
Poverty 
Monitoring 
System. 
The 
government 
has 
agreed 
with 
the 
Bank to 
carry out 
in 
fiscal 
2004 
a 
poverty 
and social 
impact 
assessment 
(PSIA)  and 
a 
poverty 
vulnerability 
study 
to 
strengthen 
analyses 
carried 
out 
so 
far 
and 
establish a stronger baseline 
for 
future 
monitoring. 
3. 
A 
solid 
track 
record 
of 
good 
macroeconomic performance 
coupled 
with 
ongoing reforms 
to 
enhance 
public 
sector 
performance and 
facilitate private 
sector 
activities 
has 
resulted 
in 
sustained 
economic 
growth 
in 
the 
range 
of 
4.0 
to 
5.8 
percent 
over 
the past 
five 
years, 
up 
from 
an 
average 
of 
1.6 
percent 
during 
the 
first 
half 
of 
the 
1990s. 
Although 
these 
reforms 
need 
to 
be 
sustained 
and 
deepened, 
the 
focus 
of 
reform 
efforts 
is 
now 
on 
aligning 
government 
policies and 
expenditure 
programs 
with 
the 
objectives 
of 
the 
PRS 
and enhancing 
the 
efficiency 
of 
public 
service 
delivery.

Page  12
A. 
THE 
LINK 
BETWEEN 
THE 
TANZANIA 
PRSC 
AND 
PROGRAMMATIC 
STRUCTURAL 
ADJUSTMENT 
CREDIT 
(PSAC) 
4. 
The 
Tanzania 
PSAC 
supports 
the 
government’s 
efforts 
to 
sustain 
macroeconomic 
stability, 
strengthen 
financial 
accountability, 
deepen 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
privatization 
program, 
improve 
competitiveness 
and transparency 
of 
markets, and 
further 
reduce 
barriers 
to 
internal 
and 
international 
trade. 
The 
Letter 
of 
Development 
Policy for 
PSACl 
identified 
a 
set 
of 
actions 
in 
these 
areas 
to 
be 
implemented over 
a 
four-year 
period. 
PSAC 
1 
supports 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
initial 
two-year 
phase 
of 
this 
program. 
Implementation 
of 
the 
reform 
agenda has 
been 
satisfactory 
and 
the 
operation 
is 
expected 
to 
close 
on 
June 
30, 
2003, 
with 
all 
agreed actions 
completed and 
all 
tranches released. 
Achievements 
include 
an increase 
in 
real per capita 
spending 
in 
the 
priority 
sectors (basic  education, 
primary 
health, water, 
rural 
roads, 
judiciary, 
and 
agricultural 
research 
and  extension) 
by 
an  estimated 
84 
percent 
over 
the 
period 
fiscal 
2001 
- 
2003,  successful 
concessioning 
of 
the 
Dar 
es 
Salaam 
Water 
and 
Sewage 
Authority 
(DAWASA)  to 
a 
private 
operator, 
making 
the 
Integrated 
Financial 
Management 
System 
(IFMS) 
fully 
operational  at 
the 
central 
government 
level, 
reforming 
legislation 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
procurement and 
land, 
liberalizing 
crop 
movements 
within 
and 
across 
international 
borders, 
rationalizing 
the 
tax  regime 
and 
a 
variety 
of 
measures 
to 
strengthen 
the 
business 
and 
regulatory 
environment, 
and make 
progress 
in 
the 
privatization 
of 
public 
enterprises 
and 
utilities. 
5. 
The 
PRSC 
for 
Tanzania 
will 
deepen 
the 
reform 
agenda 
supported 
by 
PSAC 
1 
and 
include 
new 
areas 
of 
activity, 
mainly 
aimed 
at 
scaling 
up 
growth 
and  broadening 
its 
impact 
on 
poverty 
reduction,  and 
a 
sharper 
focus 
on 
achieving 
results 
from 
public 
spending. 
The 
switch 
from 
a 
PSAC 
to 
a 
PRSC 
also 
addresses 
Box 
1: 
From 
Adjustment 
to 
Poverty 
Reduction 
Support 
The 
transition 
from 
adjustment 
lending 
to 
poverty reduction 
support 
is 
grounded 
in 
the 
adoption 
of 
the 
PRS 
as 
the 
guiding 
policy 
framework 
and  Tanzania’s 
success 
in 
achieving 
macroeconomic 
stabilization 
and 
removing 
key 
structural 
constraints 
in 
the 
economy. 
The 
PRSC 
fully 
embraces 
the 
principles 
underlying 
the 
PRS, 
which 
are 
reflected 
in 
the 
design 
of 
the  operation. 
Foremost, 
the 
PRSC 
takes 
seriously 
the 
central 
role 
of 
the 
PES 
as 
the 
guiding 
framework. 
While 
adjustment 
lending 
relied 
primarily on 
externally 
monitored, 
ex-ante 
policy 
conditionality, 
the 
PESC 
seeks 
to 
reinforce 
government 
ownership 
and 
the 
role 
of 
local 
stakeholders 
by 
emphasizing 
the 
role 
of 
local 
processes 
for 
policy 
design, 
implementation, 
and 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation. 
The 
PRSC 
fully 
reflects 
the 
importance 
of 
a transparent 
and 
participatory 
approach 
to 
policy 
making 
to 
sustain 
good 
policies 
and 
build 
institutional 
capacitv. 
several 
lessons learned 
during 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
PSAC. 
Key 
among 
these 
is 
the 
adoption 
of 
a 
single 
tranche, 
ex 
post 
conditionality 
framework, 
which 
avoids uncertainty 
in 
disbursement 
linked 
to 
within-year 
conditionality. 
The 
PSAC 
experience 
illustrated 
the 
potential 
of 
ex 
post 
conditionality 
to 
introduce 
additional  uncertainty 
into 
resource 
flows, 
often 
as 
a 
result 
of 
circumstances 
outside the 
direct 
control 
of 
government, 
such 
as 
in 
the 
case 
of 
the 
privatization 
of 
the 
Dar 
es 
Salaam 
Water 
and 
Sewage 
Authority. 
In 
addition, 
the 
PRSC approach also 
provides 
more 
flexibility 
to 
react 
to 
changing 
circumstances 
in 
the 
design 
of 
consecutive 
PRSCs; 
under the 
PSAC, 
it 
was 
difficult 
to 
make 
such adjustment 
to 
the 
agreed 
conditionality. 
6. 
Continuity 
and 
deepening 
of 
the 
PSAC 
agenda 
will 
be 
mainly 
through  supporting 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
Business 
Environment 
Strengthening 
for 
Tanzania 
(BEST) 
program and 
carrying 
over 
the 
PSAC 
components 
explicitly into 
the 
PRSC 
program. 
In 
2002, 
the government 
of 
Tanzania 
(GOT) 
approved 
a 
program 
for 
reducing 
the 
administrative 
burden 
on 
businesses 
and 
offering 
improved 
public 
services 
to 
the 
private 
sector. 
This 
program 
builds 
on 
the 
PSAC 
reform 
2 
Page  13
agenda 
by 
carrying forward 
commitments 
made 
in 
the 
PSACl 
Letter 
of 
Development 
Policy. 
The 
program 
also  incorporates 
results 
from 
a 
two-year 
consultative 
process 
under 
the 
integrated 
framework 
for 
trade 
to 
identify 
major 
constraints 
facing 
business operations 
in 
Tanzania. 
The 
core 
components 
of 
the 
five-year 
program 
aim 
at 
radical 
reduction 
of 
administrative 
complications  currently 
faced 
by 
businesses 
through 
streamlining  regulatory 
and 
licensing 
procedures, 
improving 
commercial 
dispute 
resolution, 
strengthening 
the 
operation 
of 
the 
Tanzania 
Investment 
Center 
for 
unencumbered 
facilitation 
of 
private 
investment,  and 
changing 
the 
government 
culture 
for 
more  positive  interaction 
with 
the 
private 
sector. 
Program 
implementation 
will 
be supported 
by 
a 
multidonor 
basket 
fund. 
7. 
Three 
other 
parts 
of 
the 
PSAC  reform 
agenda-efficient 
delivery 
of 
public 
services, 
improved 
public 
expenditure

and 
improved 
public

management-are 
carried 
forward 
as 
separate 
components 
of 
the 
PRSC 
program. 
The 
residual 
privatization 
and 
regulatory framework program 
will 
continue 
to 
be 
supported 
under 
the 
Bank’s 
privatization 
and

sector 
development 
project 
and 
respective sector 
projects after 
SAC 
1 
and 
PSAC 
1 
have 
successfully supported 
privatization 
of 
telecoms, 
the 
National 
Bank 
of 
Commerce, and 
nearly 
200 
commercial  production 
entities  (industrial, 
services, 
and 
agricultural); 
selection 
of 
private 
operators 
for 
the 
Dar 
es 
Salaam 
water 
supply, 
container 
terminal, 
and 
Tanzania

Corporation;  and 
setting 
up

the 
multisector umbrella  framework 
and 
respective 
laws 
for

utilities, 
transportation, telecommunications, 
civil 
aviation, 
and broadcasting. 
B. 
INNOVATIONS 
UNDER 
THE 
TANZANIA 
PRSC 
8. 
The 
innovations 
under 
the 
proposed 
operation 
are 
in 
two 
broad 
areas. 
First, 
it 
supports a 
shift 
of 
policy 
focus 
from 
mainly 
ensuring 
that 
expenditure allocations 
are 
in 
line 
with 
agreed 
priorities 
to 
putting 
greater emphasis 
on 
the 
effectiveness 
of 
expenditure 
programs 
and 
holding 
sectors 
accountable 
for 
results 
and 
outcomes 
of 
programs 
through 
a 
strong 
M&E 
system. 
The 
focus 
on 
results includes 
monitoring 
progress 
toward achieving 
the 
Millennium 
Development 
Goals 
(MDGs) 
within 
Tanzania’s 
Poverty 
Monitoring 
System. 
Second, 
it 
supports greater 
focus 
on 
pro-poor 
growth 
policy 
reforms, especially 
those 
affecting 
rural 
incomes. 
Previously, 
the focus 
was 
on 
attacking 
poverty 
through 
social 
sector 
programs, 
but 
little 
progress 
has 
been 
made 
in 
reducing income 
poverty, 
particularly 
in 
rural 
areas. 
9. 
Sectoral 
issues  are 
left 
for 
implementation 
under 
specific 
sector  programs, 
but 
the 
effectiveness 
of 
their 
spending 
and 
impact 
on 
poverty 
is 
monitored 
under 
the 
PRSC 
program. 
Sector 
programs 
are 
in 
place 
for 
the 
education,  health, 
and 
agriculture 
sectors 
and 
under 
development 
for 
the 
roads 
and water 
sectors. 
These 
sector 
programs 
enjoy 
broad 
donor support 
and 
are 
the 
government’s 
preferred 
instrument 
for 
donor  coordination. 
The 
sector 
reviews 
undertaken 
each year 
under the sector-wide 
approach 
(SWAP) 
will 
pay particular 
attention 
to 
poverty 
reduction 
targets 
and 
their 
achievements and 
not 
just 
to 
accounting 
for 
inputs 
and 
outputs 
delivered. 
A 
major 
objective 
of 
this 
framework 
is 
to 
ensure 
consistency 
between 
sector 
development programs and the PRS 
as 
well 
as 
enhance 
and 
incentivize 
capacity 
for monitoring 
results. 
To 
the 
extent 
possible, 
the 
annual 
and 
periodic 
monitoring 
indicators 
for 
the 
results 
will 
be 
calibrated 
against 
indicators 
for 
the 
MDGs. 
Feasible annual  indicators, 
as 
well 
as 
periodic 
quantitative 
indicators, 
for 
assessing 
progress 
toward 
meeting 
the 
PRS targets 
have 
been 
agreed 
after consultations among 
stakeholders, 
including 
the 
priority 
sector 
groups 
in 
Tanzania, 
in 
the 
context 
of 
the 
now 
established 
Poverty 
Monitoring 
System. 
These 
quantitative 
indicators 
are 
presented 
in 
annex 
7. 
3 
Page  14
c. 
ALIGNMENT 
WITH 
OTHER 
DEVELOPMENT 
PARTNERS 
IN 
SUPPORTING 
TANZANIA’S 
PRS 
10. 
Donor 
assistance 
plays 
an 
important 
role 
in 
supporting the  implementation 
of 
the  PRS 
because 
Tanzania’s 
own 
resources 
are 
insufficient 
to 
reach 
the 
PRS objectives.  Tanzania 
has 
made 
significant 
progress 
in 
enhancing 
its 
public 
expenditure 
management, 
including 
a 
highly 
consultative 
public 
expenditure 
review 
and 
medium-term 
expenditure 
framework (PER-MTEF) 
process, 
the  progressive 
implementation 
of 
a 
robust  integrated 
financial 
management 
system 
(IFMS), 
and 
measures 
to 
fight 
corruption. 
This 
has 
provided 
the 
impetus 
to 
a 
group 
of 
11 donors 
to 
provide 
budget support 
through 
the 
Poverty Reduction 
Budget 
Support 
(PRBS) 
facility. 
Policy 
dialogue and 
decisions 
on 
disbursements 
take 
place 
in 
the 
context 
of 
a 
common 
performance 
assessment 
framework 
(PAF) 
’ 
linked 
to 
the 
annual PRS progress reports. 
The 
proposed 
PRSC 
aims 
to 
fully 
align 
the 
Bank’s 
budget 
support 
with 
the well-established 
and effective budget 
support 
review 
and 
disbursement 
mechanisms 
of 
other 
d 
onors. 
Also, 
see 
section 
1V.C 
for 
details 
on 
alignment 
between 
Bank 
and 
other donors’ programs. 
11. 
RECENT ECONOMIC 
DEVELOPMENTS 
AND 
PROGRESS 
IN 
POVERTY 
REDUCTION 
A. 
Macroeconomic 
and 
Social Progress 
Improvement 
in 
Macroeconomic 
Performance 
1 
1. 
GDP 
growth: 
Tanzania’s 
macroeconomic performance 
has 
continued 
to 
improve 
during 
2000, 2001, 
and 2002 
(see 
annexes 
1 and 
3). 
The 
annual 
growth 
rate’ 
increased 
to 
4.9 
percent 
in 
2000, 
further 
to 
5.6 
percent 
in 
2001, 
and 
is 
projected 
at 
5.9 
percent 
in 
2002 
despite 
the 
negative 
effects 
on 
commodity 
prices 
and 
tourism 
of 
the 
slowdown 
in 
the 
world 
economy. 
The 
rise 
in 
growth 
during 
2001 
and 
2002 
was 
led 
by 
relatively 
strong performance 
in 
agriculture, 
mining, 
and  wholesale 
and 
retail 
trade. 
The 
impact 
of 
market 
reforms, 
good rainfall, 
and 
improved 
markets, 
particularly 
for  food 
crops 
in 
the 
neighboring 
countries, 
has 
combined 
to 
raise 
agriculture 
growth 
at 
6.5 
percent 
in 
2001 
and an estimated 
5.5 
percent 
in 
2002. 
Growth 
in 
the 
mining 
sector 
remained 
strong 
in 
2001 
at 
13.5 
percent, 
raising 
its 
overall contribution 
to 
GDP 
to 
2.5 
percent 
from 
negligible 
levels 
five 
years ago. 
Wholesale and 
retail 
trade 
(including 
tourism) 
grew 
at 
7.0 
percent. 
Industry 
(manufacturing, 
utilities, 
construction, 
transport, 
and 
communication) 
grew 
at an 
average 
of 
nearly 
6 percent, 
posting 
improvements 
in 
all 
subsectors 
over  1999 
and 
2000 
except 
for 
construction, 
which 
had 
peaked 
earlier 
during 
the 
height 
of 
investment 
in 
gold 
mining. 
Private 
sector 
growth, 
beyond 
mining 
and 
tourism, 
has 
been 
slower 
in 
responding 
to 
the 
liberalization. 
12. 
External 
sector: 
In 
the external 
sector, 
total 
receipts 
from 
export 
of 
goods and services 
increased 
from 
about 
US$1.33 
billion 
in 
2000 
to 
about 
US$1.56 
billion 
in 
2001,  and 
they 
are 
estimated 
to 
have 
increased 
to 
US$1.53 
billion 
in 
2002. 
The 
volume 
of 
traditional  export 
’ 
The PRSC 
policy 
matrix 
(or 
PAF) 
provides a 
substantially harmonized 
set 
of 
policy 
actions 
in 
support 
of 
the 
PRS. 
In 
the 
context 
of 
the 
PRSC, 
both 
the PRBS 
donors 
and 
the 
World 
Bank 
will 
avoid introducing 
new 
targets, 
indicators, 
or 
measures 
that 
are 
outside 
the 
PAF 
and the 
IMF 
macroeconomic 
annex. 
It 
is 
the 
intention 
of 
the parties 
to 
harmonize the 
PRS 
action 
plan 
and 
the 
PAF 
completely 
within 
three 
years. 
The 
PAF 
will 
be used 
as 
a 
tool 
for 
dialogue 
on 
PRS 
implementation 
and progress, 
and 
PAF 
actions 
will 
help 
to 
determine appropriate 
levels 
of 
future 
funding for 
the 
general budget. 
Through a 
continuous 
process, 
it will 
allow 
the government 
and 
the 
donors 
to 
assess 
progress 
toward 
agreed 
medium-term objectives 
and the 
implementation 
status 
of 
specific 
measures. 
GDP 
growth 
rate 
is 
measured 
at 
factor 
cost. 
4 
Page  15
commodities 
has 
declined, 
caused 
partly 
by 
lower 
commodity 
prices. Nonetheless, 
the 
value 
of 
merchandise exports 
grew 
by 
17 
percent 
in 
2001 
as 
a 
result 
of 
a 
sharp 
increase 
in 
nontraditional 
exports, 
mainly 
gold. 
Tanzania’s 
gold 
export 
increased 
from 
US$112.6 
million 
in 
2000 
to 
US$250.4 
million 
in 
2001. 
Imports 
grew 
by 
11.5 
percent, 
reflecting 
higher 
imports 
of 
capital 
goods 
as 
well 
as 
higher 
recorded 
imports 
of 
petroleum 
products. 
Foreign direct 
investment 
(FDI4 
had 
reached  a  peak 
in 
2000, 
with 
inflows 
of 
US$463 
million,  mainly 
on 
account 
of 
large 
investment 
in 
the 
mining 
sector. 
Since 
then, 
some 
of 
the 
largest investments 
in 
the 
mining 
sector 
have been 
completed 
and 
gold 
production 
has 
come 
on 
stream and supported  an increase 
in 
exports. 
As 
a 
consequence, 
FDI 
has 
declined 
to 
US$327 
million 
in 
2001 and 
a 
projected 
US$240 
million 
in 
2002 
and  beyond. 
Despite 
the 
decline, 
foreign 
investment 
inflows 
are 
still 
high 
compared 
with 
levels before 
1995, 
at 
less 
than 
US$50 
million 
annually, 
and compared 
with 
other 
countries 
in 
the 
region. 
The 
current 
account 
deficit 
(before  grants) 
of 
the 
balance 
of 
payments 
declined 
from 
12 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
1999 
to 
less 
than 
10 
percent 
of 
GDP 
since 2000, 
reflecting 
in 
particular 
the 
coming 
on 
stream 
of 
gold 
exports. 
These 
developments 
contributed 
to 
a 
further 
strengthening 
of 
international 
reserves. 
At 
the end 
of 
October 
2002, 
international 
reserves 
stood 
at 
US$1.3 
billion, 
equivalent 
to 
seven 
months 
of 
imports 
of 
goods and 
nonfactor 
services. 
The 
exchange 
rate 
against 
the 
U.S. 
dollar 
continued 
to 
depreciate 
between 
2000 
and 
2002. 
13. 
External 
debt 
and 
debt 
management: 
Tanzania 
reached 
the 
completion 
point 
under the 
enhanced 
Heavily 
Indebted Poor 
Countries 
Initiative 
(HIPC) 
in 
November 
200 
1. 
Along 
with 
the 
HIPC 
assistance 
from 
IDA 
and the 
International 
Monetary 
Fund 
(IMF), 
which 
together 
account 
for 
more 
than 
40 
percent 
of 
the 
total, 
the 
Paris 
Club 
creditors 
provided 
another 
40 percent 
by 
granting 
a stock 
of 
debt 
reduction 
on 
Cologne 
terms 
in 
January 2002. Issues 
remain 
in 
obtaining 
comparable treatment 
from 
non-Paris 
Club 
creditors. 
Tanzania 
has 
also 
taken 
significant 
steps 
to 
deal 
with 
its 
domestic 
debt, 
particularly 
by 
reducing 
its 
arrears 
with 
the 
private 
sector 
and 
private 
external  commercial 
debt.  Tanzania’s 
debt 
management 
is 
strengthened 
through 
the 
recent 
adoption 
of 
the 
National 
Debt 
Strategy and 
the 
amendment 
of 
the 
Loans, Guarantees 
and 
Grants 
Act 
of 
1974. 
The 
strategy emphasizes 
avoiding 
a relapse 
to 
the 
situation 
of 
unsustainable debt. 
Among 
the 
important 
elements 
under 
this 
strategy 
are 
the 
establishment 
of 
a 
National 
Debt 
Management 
Committee 
and 
the 
vesting 
of 
the  government 
to 
raise 
money 
by 
loan,  issue 
guarantees, 
and 
accept grants 
on 
behalf 
of 
the government 
solely 
in 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance. 
14. 
Debt 
sustainability: 
Tanzania’s 
public 
and 
publicly 
guaranteed 
external 
debt 
is 
sustainable, 
having 
completed 
all 
required conditions 
and obtained irrevocable 
debt 
relief 
under 
the enhanced 
HIPC 
Initiative 
(amounting 
to 
US$3 
billion). 
Debt 
relief 
granted 
to 
Tanzania 
under 
the 
enhanced 
HIPC 
Initiative 
(decision 
point 
2000 
and 
completion 
point 
2001) 
was 
crucial 
in 
achieving 
debt 
sustainability 
and 
enhancing 
the 
resource 
base 
that 
can 
be 
used 
in 
the 
pursuit 
of 
the 
MDGs. 
Sound macroeconomic and structural 
policies 
created 
the 
basis 
for qualifying for 
debt 
relief. 
The 
revised 
net 
present 
value (NPV) 
of 
the 
debt-to-export 
ratio 
for 
2001/02 
was 127.8 
percent, 
which 
is 
considerably 
lower 
than 
projected 
during 
the 
decision 
and 
completion 
points. 
The 
improvement 
in 
debt 
sustainability 
reflects 
not only 
higher 
exports 
and 
lower 
than 
expected 
new 
borrowings, 
but 
also 
additional 
debt 
relief from 
Paris 
Club 
creditors 
beyond 
the 
HIPC 
Initiative. 
By 
201 
1/12, 
the 
NPV 
of 
the 
debt-to-export 
ratio 
will 
decline 
further 
to 
117 percent; 
debt 
as 
a 
proportion 
of 
GDP 
is 
projected 
to 
decline 
moderately, 
fkom 
18.3 
percent 
from 
2001/02 
to 
17.7 
percent 
in 
201 
1/12, 
and 
the

of

revenue

155

1/12.

15. 
Fiscal 
management: 
Continued 
conservative 
fiscal 
policy 
management,  supported 
by 
cautious monetary 
policy, contributed 
to 
a 
decline 
in 
inflation 
from 
7.0 
percent 
at 
the 
end 
of 
1999 
to 
4.9 
percent 
by 
the 
end 
of 
2001 
and 
further 
to 
4.4 
percent 
by 
the 
end 
of 
September 
2002. 
The 
deficit-to-GDP4 
outturn (including 
grants) 
in 
200 1/02 
was  1.1 
percent and 
represents 
a 
smaller 
deficit 
than 
budgeted. 
It 
is 
projected 
to 
be 
4.1 
percent 
in 
2002/03 
because 
of 
a substantial increase 
in 
priority

and 
a 
better 
capture 
of 
foreign-financed 
development expenditure 
in 
the 
budget. 
It 
is 
projected 
to 
decline 
to 
3.6 
percent 
in 
2003/04. 
Domestic 
revenue 
and

expenditure 
ratios 
to 
GDP 
in

were

rising 
from 
12.1

12.2

from

respectively. 
The

is

to 
increase 
to 
12.3

in

further 
to 
12.8

in

The 
government 
is

to 
increasing 
its

as

to

The

as

to

import 
regime. 
16. 
Fiscal 
sustainability: 
Over 
the 
past 
three 
years, 
the 
level 
and 
composition 
of 
foreign 
aid 
to 
Tanzania 
has 
changed 
in 
a 
number 
of 
important 
ways. Grants 
and soft loans 
(net) 
to 
Tanzania 
have increased 
from 
4 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
fiscal 
1997 
to 
5.9 percent 
in 
fiscal 
2002. Part 
of 
the 
increase 
in 
fiscal 
2002 
is 
attributed 
to 
better 
recording 
of 
aid. 
As 
a 
proportion 
of 
the 
total 
budget, 
official 
development 
assistance 
(ODA) 
accounts 
for 
about 
40 
percent, 
registering 
a 
significant 
rise 
in 
fiscal 2001 
and 
fiscal 
2002. 
Grant 
financing 
was 
relatively 
stable at 
around 
4 
percent 
of 
GDP, 
and 
loan 
disbursements 
recovered 
from 
a 
relatively 
low 
level 
of 
1.6 
percent 
in 
fiscal 
1999 
to 
2.2 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
fiscal 
2000 
and 
fiscal 
2001. 
Inflows from 
concessional  loans 
net 
of 
amortization 
have increased 
continuously 
over 
the 
past 
three 
years, 
from 
0.4 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
fiscal 
1999 
to 
1.1 
percent 
in 
fiscal 
2001. 
Until 
fiscal 
1999, 
project 
support was 
increasing and 
program 
support 
was  decreasing. 
This 
trend 
has 
been 
reversed 
in 
the 
past 
two 
years, 
with 
a 
decline 
in 
project 
support 
and 
an 
increase 
in 
program 
support, 
which 
is 
even 
more 
pronounced 
when 
HIPC 
debt 
relief 
is 
taken 
into 
account 
as 
a 
form 
of 
program 
support. 
Program support 
provides 
the 
flexibility 
to 
fully 
integrate recurrent 
and 
development 
spending 
under 
the 
MTEF, 
and 
it 
will 
be 
important 
that 
adequate 
capacity 
is 
developed 
to 
ensure 
proper 
allocation 
of 
both 
tax 
and 
program 
aid 
resources 
between development 
and 
recurrent 
expenditures. 
17. 
The overall 
resource envelope 
for 
supporting 
poverty reduction 
in 
Tanzania 
is 
still 
far 
short 
of 
the 
requirements 
for 
achieving 
the 
MDGs. 
This 
notwithstanding, 
there 
are three 
important 
caveats 
emerging 
from 
the 
rising 
trend 
of 
higher 
dependence 
on 
ODA 
and 
the 
shift 
in 
composition 
of 
aid 
toward program 
support. 
First, 
funds 
from 
program 
support 
are 
fully 
fungible 
to 
funds 
from 
domestic 
revenue.  Because increased 
program 
support 
lessens 
the 
pressure 
to 
increase 
domestic 
revenue, 
appropriate  incentives  should 
be 
put 
in 
place 
to 
ensure 
that 
an 
equitable 
and 
growth-supporting 
tax 
system 
is 
in 
place, 
which 
in 
the 
medium 
to 
long 
term 
will 
have 
the 
capacity 
to 
generate 
sufficient 
funds 
to 
lessen 
the 
dependence 
on 
program 
support. 
Second, 
in 
the 
past, 
program 
support 
typically 
showed  a 
higher 
degree 
of 
variability 
and 
unpredictability 
than 
domestic revenue. 
Given 
the 
high 
cost 
that 
resource 
variability 
imposes 
on 
economic 
management, 
it 
will 
be 
important 
to 
develop 
aid 
mechanisms 
that 
allow 
for 
stable 
and 
predictable 
aid 
disbursements 
and 
prevent 
sudden 
changes 
in 
the 
volume 
of 
aid. 
Third, 
to 
the 
extent  that  budget 
support  finances  a  greater 
share 
of 
nontradables 
(eg, 
teacher 
salaries, 
The fiscal 
ratios 
are 
calculated 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
GDP 
at 
market 
prices. 
6 
Page  17
construction 
of 
classrooms) 
than 
does 
project 
support, a 
shift 
toward 
program 
support leads 
to 
upward 
pressure (appreciation) 
of 
the 
exchange rate, 
with 
potential 
implications 
for 
Tanzania's 
international 
competitiveness. 
Reduction 
in 
Poverty 
18. 
With 
the 
adoption 
of 
the 
National 
Poverty Eradication 
Strategy (NPES) 
and, 
in 
particular, 
the 
PRS, 
poverty 
reduction 
has 
become 
the 
overarching 
objective 
of 
public 
policy, 
actions, 
and 
M&E. 
19. 
A 
comprehensive 
HBS 
was 
carried out 
in 
fiscal 
2001 and 
the 
results 
were 
released 
in 
April 
2002. Other 
baseline data 
for 
monitoring 
poverty 
are 
provided 
by 
the 
Tanzania 
Reproductive 
and 
Child 
Health 
Survey 
(1999) 
and 
a 
labor force survey 
(2000/01).  Results 
of 
the 
surveys 
reflect 
the 
positive 
economic 
developments, 
particularly 
in 
the 
second 
half 
of 
the 
1990s. 
Although 
Tanzania 
experienced economic 
difficulties 
and 
worsening 
poverty 
in 
the early 
1990s, 
the 
benefits 
of 
the 
more 
recent 
growth 
have 
begun 
to 
reverse 
that 
trend. 
Food 
poverty 
has 
declined 
from 
21.6 
percent 
to 
18.7 
percent, and 
basic 
needs 
poverty 
decreased 
from 
38.6 percent 
to 
35.7 
percent. 
20. 
The 
decline 
in 
income poverty 
is 
corroborated 
by 
improvements 
in 
other dimensions 
of 
poverty 
covered 
by 
the 
survey. 
Most 
notably, 
improvements have 
been 
registered 
in 
the 
quality 
of 
and 
access 
to 
drinking 
water 
in 
rural 
areas, 
a 
relatively 
sharp 
rise 
in 
the 
use 
of 
modern 
construction 
materials 
for 
housing 
(urban and rural), 
and 
increased 
access 
to 
electricity, 
markets, 
and 
public 
transport.  There 
has 
also 
been 
a 
rise 
in 
asset 
holding, 
mostly 
consumer  durables. 
Figures 
1 
and 
2 present 
the 
trends 
in 
basic 
poverty 
indicators 
in 
the 
last 
decade. 
Figure

Percentage 
of 
the 
Population 
below 
the 
Basic Needs 
Poverty

and 
2000/01

1. 
The 
government 
has 
set 
up 
an 
institutional 
framework 
for 
poverty monitoring, 
based 
on 
the 
recently 
prepared 
poverty 
monitoring 
master 
plan. 
The 
government 
is 
also 
considering the most 
effective 
way 
to 
integrate the 
MDGs 
into 
an 
updated 
PRS and 
poverty 
monitoring 
systems. 
Such 
integration 
will 
take 
the current 
poverty 
status 
as 
the 
baseline and 
calibrate 
the 
goals 
consistent 
with 
implementation 
capacity. 
22. 
Tanzania 
continues 
to 
focus 
on 
growth 
and 
antipoverty 
measures 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
its 
poverty 
reduction 
strategy 
through 
a 
variety 
of 
sectoral 
and 
cross-sectoral programs, 
as 
well 
as 
through 
the 
national 
budget 
supported 
by 
a 
participatory 
PER-MTEF 
process. 
The 
expenditure 
plan 
in 
the budget 
is 
closely 
aligned 
with 
the 
core 
PRS 
programs: 
education, health, water, 
rural 
roads, 
agriculture, 
and governance. 
Tanzania protects 
budgeted spending 
in 
PRS 
priority 
areas 
from 
cuts 
during 
budget 
execution. 
In 
fiscal 
2002, 
all priority 
spending 
programs and 
locally 
financed 
development 
spending 
were 
executed 
as 
budgeted. 
The 
budget 
for 
fiscal 
2003 
reflects 
Tanzania’s 
continued 
focus 
on 
implementing 
the 
PRS. 
B. 
Progress 
in 
Structural 
and 
Social 
Reforms 
23. 
Starting 
in 
the late 
1980s (and 
pursued 
with 
renewed 
vigor 
since 
the 
mid-l990s), 
Tanzania 
has 
implemented 
a 
broad 
structural  and 
social 
reform 
agenda. 
After 
the 
initial 
objectives 
of 
macrostabilization 
and 
removal 
of 
key 
distortions 
in 
the 
economy had 
been 
attained, 
reforms 
have 
more recently 
focused 
on improving 
economic 
governance, 
providing 
an 
enabling environment 
for 
private 
sector 
activities, and enhancing 
public 
service 
delivery 
for 
poverty 
reduction. 
Economic 
Governance 
24. 
Tanzania 
has 
initiated 
comprehensive 
reforms 
to 
improve 
economic 
governance. 
Reforms 
in 
the 
area 
of 
public 
financial 
management 
include 
the 
implementation 
of 
an 
IFMS, 
the 
adoption 
of 
an 
inclusive 
PER-MTEF 
process, 
and 
the 
revision 
of 
the 
public 
finance management 
and 
the 
public 
procurement 
acts. 
The 
first 
phase 
of 
civil 
service 
reform, 
downsizing 
the 
civil 
service 
and 
bringing 
the 
wage 
bill 
under 
control, 
has 
been 
completed. 
Reforms 
are 
now 
in 
their 
second 
phase 
8 
Page  19
and 
will 
increasingly 
focus 
on 
public 
sector 
performance. 
A 
local 
government 
reform 
program 
aims 
at 
strengthening 
capacities 
of 
the 
local 
authorities, 
which 
bear 
the 
primary 
responsibility 
for 
basic service 
delivery, 
such 
as 
basic 
education, 
primary 
health, water, 
and 
rural 
roads, 
in 
priority 
sectors 
under 
the 
PRS. 
Following 
up 
on 
the 
recommendations 
of 
the 
Warioba 
report, 
which 
documented deeply 
entrenched 
corruption 
in 
the 
public 
sector, 
a 
national action 
plan for 
the 
control 
of 
corruption 
and 
sector-specific 
anticorruption 
plans 
are 
being 
implemented. 
The 
government 
has 
set 
up 
a 
good 
governance 
coordination 
unit 
in 
the 
chief 
secretary’s 
office 
and 
has 
agreed 
to 
implement 
a 
legal 
sector 
reform 
program. 
Progress 
in 
reducing 
corruption 
is 
partly 
mirrored 
by 
the 
improvement 
in 
Tanzania’s 
relative 
position 
on 
the 
Transparency 
International 
corruption 
perception 
index, 
from 
the 
9 
1st 
position 
in 
2000 
to 
7 1 
st 
position 
(out 
of 
106 countries) 
in 
2002. 
25. 
The 
government, 
working 
with 
development 
partners, 
is 
in 
the 
process 
of 
finalizing 
a 
framework 
for  monitoring 
progress 
in 
improving 
governance. 
The 
framework 
maps 
priority 
actions 
to 
be 
taken 
to 
achieve goals 
set 
out 
in 
the 
PRS 
and 
identifies indicators 
for monitoring 
implementation 
and 
results. 
The 
framework 
cuts 
across 
the 
key 
areas 
of 
action 
and 
seeks 
greater 
coherence 
across 
them. 
An 
Enabling 
Environment 
for 
Private 
Sector 
Activities 
26. 
The 
government 
has 
shown 
a 
strong 
commitment 
to 
withdraw 
from 
commercial activities 
and 
define 
its 
role 
as 
an enabler 
of 
private 
sector 
activities. 
Key 
achievements 
in 
this 
area 
include 
the 
privatization  or  liquidation 
of 
more  than 
80 
percent 
of 
parastatals. 
This 
includes  the 
privatization 
of 
the 
fixed-net 
telephone 
operation 
with 
a 
parallel 
liberalization 
of 
the 
wireless 
telephone  market, 
which 
resulted 
in 
a 
rapid 
increase 
in 
the 
number 
of 
subscribers. 
Similarly, 
government 
has 
disinvested 
its 
interests 
in 
and 
liberalized 
the 
banking 
sector, 
which 
has 
experienced 
a sharp increase 
in 
the 
number 
of 
private 
sector 
banks-with 
23 
now 
operating 
in 
the 
country. 
Recent 
privatizations include 
Air 
Tanzania, Tanga 
Cement, 
and 
private 
operation 
of 
the 
container 
terminal. 
27. 
With 
a 
large 
number 
of 
state-owned 
enterprises 
in 
the 
manufacturing 
sector 
having 
been 
divested 
or 
liquidated, 
the 
focus 
of 
the 
privatization 
program 
has 
now 
moved 
to 
the 
restructuring 
and 
divestiture 
of 
large 
utilities, 
especially 
power, 
water, 
and 
key 
transport 
services, 
and the 
development 
of 
an 
appropriate 
regulatory framework 
to 
govern the operation 
of 
these 
liberalized 
sectors 
in 
a 
competitive 
environment. 
The 
target 
of 
this 
program 
is, 
among other 
things, 
to 
reduce 
the 
cost 
of 
doing 
business 
in 
the 
country  through 
the 
provision 
of 
affordable 
and 
reliable 
infrastructure 
services. 
28. 
A 
private 
operator 
of 
the 
Dar 
es 
Salaam 
Water 
and 
Sewage 
Authority 
(DAWASA) 
was 
selected 
and  approved 
by 
the  cabinet 
in 
December 
2002. 
The 
process 
of 
selecting 
a 
private 
operator 
for 
the  Tanzania 
Railways 
Corporation 
is 
at 
an 
advanced 
stage. 
The 
process 
of 
unbundling 
the Tanzania 
Electricity 
Supply 
Company 
(TANESCO) 
into 
autonomous 
commercial 
entities 
was 
begun 
in 
September 
2001. 
Legislation 
was 
passed 
in 
April 
2001 
to 
establish the 
Surface 
and 
Marine 
Transport 
Regulatory 
Authority 
(SUMATRA) 
and the 
Energy 
and 
Water 
Utilities 
Regulatory 
Authority 
(EWURA). 
Responsibility 
for 
regulation 
of 
safety and 
quality 
of 
petroleum 
products, 
railways,  and 
ports, 
and 
the 
location 
of 
telecommunication,  aviation,  and 
broadcasting 
into 
the 
new 
multisector  regulatory 
framework, 
remain 
to 
be 
decided.  These 
measures 
were 
supported 
by 
PSACl 
and 
benefited 
from 
IDA’S 
technical 
assistance 
under the 
Privatization 
and 
Private 
Sector 
Development 
Project. 
9 
Page  20
29. 
A 
key 
government 
strategy 
is 
to 
improve 
the 
investment 
climate 
to 
stimulate 
more 
rapid 
expansion 
of 
local 
and 
foreign private 
investment. 
A 
notable 
success 
in 
this 
area 
was 
the 
setup 
of 
a 
policy 
framework 
for 
the 
mining 
sector 
in 
the 
mid-l990s, 
which 
has 
since experienced 
two- 
digit 
growth 
rates. 
Other 
important 
measures 
include 
the 
revision 
of 
legislation, 
including 
the 
company law, 
and 
the 
liberalization 
of 
capital 
markets. 
The 
Tanzania 
Investment 
Center 
has 
been 
restructured 
and 
competitively 
restaffed 
to 
transform 
it 
into 
an 
effective 
service 
center 
for 
investors. 
To 
enable 
easier 
access 
to 
credit 
and 
the 
use 
of 
land 
as 
collateral, 
land 
regulations 
were 
issued 
and 
gazetted 
in 
May 
2001, 
bringing 
the 
1999 
Land 
Act 
Number 
4 
and 
the 
Village 
Land 
Act 
Number 
5 
into 
effect. 
The 
government 
is 
now 
in 
the 
process 
of 
further 
reviewing 
the 
new 
land laws 
to 
address 
residual 
concerns 
of 
investors  and 
financial 
intermediaries,  and 
it 
is 
also 
considering 
measures 
for 
more effective application 
of 
the 
new 
laws. 
The 
opening 
up 
of 
the 
Dar 
es 
Salaam 
stock 
exchange 
to 
foreign 
investors 
has 
been 
approved 
and 
is 
expected 
to 
become 
effective 
in 
2003. 
30. 
In 
April 
2002, 
the government 
and 
the 
private 
sector 
launched 
a 
framework 
for 
public- 
private 
sector 
partnership 
under 
the 
Tanzania 
National 
Business 
Council 
(TNBC). 
The 
council 
is 
chaired 
by 
the 
president, and 
the 
chair 
of 
the 
Private 
Sector 
Foundation 
serves 
as 
the 
council’s 
vice 
chairman. 
Within 
this 
framework, 
the 
council 
has 
organized 
two 
investor 
roundtables 
for 
local 
and 
international 
investors. 
The International Investor 
Roundtable 
(IRT), 
made 
up 
of 
global 
corporate 
executives 
(mostly 
CEOs), 
advises 
the 
president 
on 
improvement 
of 
the 
investment 
climate 
in 
Tanzania. 
A 
mechanism 
has 
been 
put 
in 
place 
to monitor 
implementation 
of 
follow-up 
actions 
drawn 
from 
advice 
given 
during 
the 
roundtable 
meetings. 
The 
roundtable 
provides 
peer 
pressure 
for 
speeding 
up 
implementation 
of 
agreed 
reforms 
in 
addition 
to 
sharing 
international 
experience 
in 
addressing constraints 
to 
private 
investment. 
The 
World 
Bank 
provides support 
to 
both 
the 
IRT 
and the 
TNBC. 
Improved 
Delivery 
of 
Public 
Services 
31. 
In 
addition 
to 
the 
overall 
measures 
to 
improve 
economic 
governance, 
public 
service 
delivery 
is 
being 
enhanced 
by 
establishing and 
aligning 
sector 
development 
programs 
with 
the 
PRS 
for 
such 
key 
sectors 
such 
as 
health, education, 
agriculture, 
and roads 
and according 
them 
priority 
budget 
formulation 
and implementation. 
32. 
Education: 
Progress 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
Education 
Sector 
Development Program 
(ESDP) 
includes 
increased 
budgetary  allocations 
to 
education, 
especially 
primary 
education, 
abolition 
of 
school 
fees 
in 
2001, establishment 
of 
the 
Education 
Fund 
to 
support 
children 
from 
very 
poor 
families, 
and, 
starting 
in 
school year 
2002, 
the 
introduction 
of 
a 
capitation  grant 
of 
US$10 
per 
pupil 
to 
cover 
expenditures 
on 
textbooks, 
other  teaching 
and 
learning 
materials, 
school 
operation 
and administration, 
and cluster-based teacher 
professional 
development. 
The 
commencement 
of 
the 
Primary 
Education Development Program 
in 
fiscal 
200 
1/02 
has 
had 
a 
particularly 
significant impact 
on 
reversing 
the 
decline 
in 
school enrollment. 
The 
second PRS 
progress 
report 
shows 
that 
the 
gross 
enrollment 
ratio 
(GER) 
reached 
100 
percent 
in 
2002, 
up 
from 
78  percent 
in 
1990 
and  surpassing the  PRS 
target 
of 
85 
percent. 
Net

in

rose 
from 
65

in 
2000 
to 
85

in

also surpassing 
the 
PRS 
medium-

of 
70 
percent. Results

2000/01 
HBS 
indicate 
that

of

now 
exceeds 
that 
of 
boys. 
Apart

to 
abolishing

by

supported

all

and  communities, 
including

of

classrooms,

of

teachers, 
and improvement 
in 
nutrition 
in 
school programs. 
However, disparities 
across 
regions  and 
districts 
are 
wide, 
with 
the 
poorest 
registering 
a 
GER 
of 
about 
80 
percent. 
The 
challenge 
of 
improving 
the 
quality 
of

education-which 
is 
needed 
to 
reduce 
the 
10 
Page  21
relatively 
high 
illiteracy

about 
25 
percent-is

because

are 
“overageyy 
and

pass 
rate

36. 
HIVMIDS: 
The 
fight 
against 
HIV/AIDS 
has 
been 
intensified, 
and 
the 
act 
establishing 
the 
Tanzania 
Commission 
on 
AIDS 
(TACAIDS) 
was 
passed 
by 
parliament 
in 
October 
2001. 
HIV/AIDS 
has 
been 
included 
in 
PER/MTEF 
process 
HIV/AIDS 
activities 
mainstreamed 
in 
all 
sector 
HIV/AIDS 
campaign covered 
all 
district 
TACAIDS 
is 
now 
operational 
following 
completion 
of 
structures 
and 
recruitment 
of 
key 
staff 
Formation 
of 
a  special 
DAC 
HIV/AIDS 
to 
work 
with 
the 
government 
to 
address 
HIV/AIDS 
issue 
Members 
of 
the 
commission 
have 
been 
appointed 
and 
TACAIDS 
is 
now 
in 
the 
process 
of 
being 
staffed. 
The 
new 
commission 
is 
the central 
coordinating 
institution 
for 
multisectoral 
response 
to 
HIV/AIDS 
in 
Tanzania. 
All 
districts 
are 
now 
covered 
by 
an 
HIV/AIDS 
awareness 
campaign, and 
each 
district 
has 
prepared 
its

plan. 
The

is 
in 
the 
process 
of 
preparing 
a 
program 
to 
fund 
scaling 
up 
and 
intensified 
action 
by

and

to 
combat 
infection 
and

of

Provisions 
have been

2003

to

and

C. 
Emerging 
Issues 
and Challenges 
for 
the 
Medium 
Term 
3 
7. 
Notwithstanding 
the 
progress 
discussed  earlier, 
poverty 
in 
Tanzania 
remains 
at 
unacceptably 
high 
levels 
and 
the 
HIV/AIDS 
pandemic 
poses 
a 
major 
threat 
to 
the 
achievements 
made 
on 
all 
fronts. 
More 
than 
35 
percent 
of 
Tanzanians cannot 
afford 
basic 
needs, 
and 
nearly 
18 
percent cannot 
afford 
the 
food 
required 
for 
healthy 
living. 
Despite 
improvements, 
53 
percent 
of 
rural 
Tanzanians 
still 
do 
not 
have 
access 
to 
protected 
water, 
and 
more than 
50 percent 
of 
them 
have 
to 
fetch 
water 
from 
sources 
more 
than 
a 
kilometer 
away. 
Ninety 
percent 
of 
Tanzanians 
do 
not 
have 
access 
to 
electricity 
or 
gas, 
and 
one-fourth 
of 
the 
population 
still 
has 
to 
travel more than 
six 
kilometers 
to 
a 
health 
center 
or 
dispensary. 
Although 
there 
have 
been 
sharp 
improvements 
in 
the 
last 
decade, 
the 
majority 
of 
Tanzanians 
still 
use 
nondurable materials 
to 
construct 
houses. 
38. 
HIV 
infection 
rates 
continue 
to 
rise, 
and, 
according 
to 
the 
latest 
estimates, 
about 
12 
percent 
of 
Tanzania’s 
adult 
population 
is 
HIV 
positive. 
The 
infection 
rate 
is 
highest among 
young 
adults 
aged 
19 
to 
25, 
which 
will 
have 
dire 
consequences 
for 
the 
productive labor 
force  and 
hence 
growth. 
Estimates indicate that 
Tanzania’s 
GDP could 
be 
15-20 
percent 
lower 
by 
20 
15 
as 
a 
result 
of 
the 
HIV/AIDS 
pandemic. 
The 
burden 
on both 
public 
and 
private 
resources 
for 
care 
of 
patients 
and 
orphans 
is 
large  and 
diverts 
scarce 
resources 
from 
support 
of 
economic 
progress  and 
improved 
livelihoods. 
Pockets 
of 
success 
in 
containing 
HIV/AIDS 
have 
occurred 
in 
Tanzania 
where 
community-based programs were 
implemented, 
But 
these 
programs 
should 
be 
scaled 
up. 
A 
key 
lesson 
from 
these experiences 
is 
that 
when 
communities 
are 
involved 
and 
interested, 
success 
is 
more 
likely. 
Government 
support  can become 
more 
cost-effective  and 
sustainable 
through 
energizing 
community 
efforts. 
12 
Page  23
39. 
As 
Tanzania 
prepares 
to 
face 
these 
daunting 
challenges 
under 
the 
auspices 
of 
the 
PRS, 
the 
issues 
identified 
in 
the 
PRS 
remain very 
much 
in 
focus and 
prompt 
continued 
effort 
to 
address 
them 
to 
make durable 
progress 
in 
poverty 
reduction. 
These 
issues 
go 
beyond 
the 
earnest 
effort 
now 
under 
way 
to 
improve 
social 
well-being 
by 
focusing 
on 
social 
sectors. 
Drawing from 
analytic 
work 
and a 
broad 
range 
of 
discussions 
with 
the 
government and other 
stakeholders, 
the 
main 
concerns 
raised relate 
to 
the 
following 
six 
areas: 
Increasing 
durable 
growth 
and  spreading 
its 
impact 
is 
a 
central 
concern 
and 
has 
become 
a 
test 
of 
whether 
the 
reforms 
have 
been 
worthwhile. 
Private 
sector 
development 
(PSD) 
has 
been 
identified 
as 
the 
leader 
of 
growth. 
The 
public 
sector 
can 
play 
a 
pivotal 
role 
in 
scaling 
up 
growth 
through predictably 
providing 
an 
enabling 
policy 
and 
institutional 
environment 
for 
the 
development 
of 
private 
initiative, 
more 
broadly, 
and 
private 
investment, 
in 
particular. 
In 
combination 
with 
ongoing efforts 
to 
enhance 
human 
capabilities 
for 
production 
and enterprise, 
and 
measures 
to 
lower 
the 
cost 
of 
doing 
business 
(infrastructure 
services 
and 
property 
rights), 
a robust 
private 
sector 
investment 
response 
in 
large-, 
medium-, 
and 
small-scale 
enterprises 
is 
key 
to 
expanding 
productive 
capacity 
and 
the 
ability 
of 
the 
economy 
to gainfully 
engage 
its 
competencies. Therefore, 
improving 
the investment 
climate 
is 
essential 
to 
success. 
The 
majority 
of 
the 
poor 
in 
Tanzania depend 
on 
agriculture, 
small-scale 
enterprise, 
and the 
informal 
sector 
for 
their 
livelihood. 
These sectors 
have also 
increasingly 
been 
providing 
work for 
both 
the 
rapidly 
expanding 
population 
of 
young 
Tanzanians 
and 
women. 
Implementation 
of 
the 
rural 
development 
strategy 
and, 
within 
it, 
the 
Agriculture 
Sector 
Development 
Program 
(ASDP), 
on 
the 
one 
hand, 
and 
promotion 
of 
investment 
in 
small 
and 
medium 
enterprises 
(SMEs) 
and 
informal 
sector 
activities, 
on 
the 
other, 
will 
help 
to 
combat 
both 
rural 
and 
urban poverty. 
In 
both 
respects, 
the 
public 
sector’s 
primary role 
is 
to 
enable 
private 
initiative 
and 
reduce 
the 
cost 
of 
such 
initiative 
through 
well-targeted policies, 
facilitating 
access 
to 
finance 
and 
knowledge, 
and 
promoting 
the 
emergence 
of 
an 
institutional 
framework that 
protects 
property 
rights 
and 
reduces 
the 
cost and 
risk 
of 
doing 
business. 
In 
the 
rapidly 
globalizing 
economy, 
improved 
competitiveness 
of 
Tanzanian 
enterprise 
is 
key 
to 
exploiting 
larger 
world 
markets  and 
pursuing 
a 
risk-reducing 
diversification 
of 
the 
Tanzanian economy. Measures 
to 
reduce 
impediments 
to 
export 
and 
favoring 
a 
diversified 
export 
orientation 
are 
fundamental 
for 
success. 
Although 
substantial 
progress 
has 
been 
made 
in 
reducing direct 
export 
controls, 
there 
is 
need 
to 
further 
reduce 
nontariff 
and domestic trade 
barriers 
and 
improve 
export 
competencies 
and 
access 
to 
information 
on 
market 
opportunities. 
FDI 
aids  such 
access 
and 
contributes 
to 
productivity 
growth. 
The 
Foreign 
Investment 
Advisory 
Service 
(FIAS) 
recently carried 
out 
an 
assessment 
and 
identified 
priority 
measures 
for 
enhancing 
competitiveness 
in 
Tanzania. 
A 
Bank 
study 
on 
rural 
development 
has 
identified 
the 
key 
impediments 
to 
agriculture exports 
in 
which 
Tanzania should 
have 
comparative 
advantage  and 
measures 
to 
remove 
those 
impediments. 
The 
study 
also 
identifies 
some 
of 
the 
key 
issues 
to 
be 
addressed 
for 
effective implementation 
of 
the 
recently 
completed 
rural 
development 
strategy. 
With 
support 
mainly 
from 
the 
U.K. 
Department 
for 
International Development 
(DFID), 
the 
Tanzanian government 
has 
identified 
strategic 
policy, 
institutional, 
and 
technical 
assistance 
needs 
for improving 
the 
business 
environment 
in 
Tanzania 
under 
the 
BEST 
program. 
In 
combination 
with 
the 
assessment 
of 
the 
main 
constraints 
faced 
by 
SMEs 
and 
the 
required 
actions 
for 
dealing 
with 
them, 
a 
set 
of 
updated 
priority 
measures 
has 
been 
identified. 
In 
addition, 
both 
the 
local 
and 
the 
international 
investor roundtables 
held 
in 
2002 
identified 
the 
Page  24
key 
impediments 
to 
private 
sector 
growth 
in 
Tanzania. 
The 
most important among 
these 
include 
security 
of 
property, 
transparency  and 
predictability 
of 
the 
business 
environment  (particularly 
in 
agriculture), 
cost 
of 
infrastructure 
services, 
and 
availability 
of 
skilled 
staff. 
(d) 
Vulnerability 
to 
risks 
in 
earnings, 
particularly 
for 
the 
majority 
of 
the 
poor 
who 
are 
dependent 
on primary 
agricultural 
export 
products, 
is 
another 
emerging 
concern. 
Arguments 
in 
favor 
of 
price 
support, controls,  and 
subsidy 
programs 
are 
reemerging 
as 
steep 
price  fluctuations 
for 
key 
primary 
exports 
expose 
poor 
smallholders 
to 
income  vulnerability. 
Promarket 
responses-for 
example, 
use 
of 
hedging 
where 
products’ 
forward 
markets 
are 
available 
or 
markets 
for 
risk 
insurance-need 
to 
be 
quickly 
developed. Otherwise, 
pressure 
will 
intensify 
for 
the 
reversal 
of 
marketing 
reforms 
that 
have 
been 
painfully 
undertaken over 
the 
last 
15 
years. 
(e) 
Although 
progress 
has 
been 
made 
in 
reorienting 
the 
allocation 
of 
public 
resources 
(own 
and 
from 
external 
sources) 
toward 
the 
priority 
sectors, 
which 
are 
predominantly 
social 
sectors, 
attention should 
now 
increasingly 
turn 
toward 
improving 
efficiency 
in 
their 
application,  increasing 
efficacy 
in 
converting 
these 
resources 
and 
efforts 
into 
desired 
services, 
and 
raising 
the 
impact 
of 
improved 
service 
delivery 
on 
the 
livelihoods 
of 
Tanzanians. 
This 
in 
turn 
provides 
a 
enduring 
motivation to 
sustain the 
reform 
effort 
and 
improvement 
in 
public 
service 
delivery. 
To 
that 
end, 
critical 
measures 
include 
those 
that target 
improved 
public 
financial 
management, 
contain 
leakages 
in 
the 
flow 
of 
resources 
through  various  levels 
of 
government 
to 
service 
delivery 
units, 
strengthen 
accountability 
for 
means 
and 
results 
of 
poverty 
reduction 
measures, 
and 
monitor 
and 
incentivize 
public 
service 
both 
professionally 
and 
financially. 
These 
fall 
under 
the 
rubric 
of 
economic 
governance. 
As 
the 
next 
round 
of 
elections 
approaches, 
concerns  about 
the 
durability 
of 
commitment 
to 
reforms 
will 
mount. 
To 
ensure 
the 
sustainability 
of 
reform 
achievements 
of 
the 
current 
government, 
legal 
and 
institutional 
changes 
are 
necessary 
to 
root 
these 
reforms 
deeply 
in 
both 
attitude 
and  practice. 
The 
Accountability, 
Transparency,  and 
Integrity 
Project 
(ATIP) 
is 
preparing 
a 
program 
to 
support 
Tanzania’s 
efforts 
to 
consolidate 
the 
institutional 
and 
legal framework. 
Assuring 
investors 
that 
the 
changes 
made 
are 
durable 
is 
a 
major 
challenge 
in 
scaling 
up 
investor 
response. 
14 
Page  25
111. 
THE 
TANZANIA 
PRS 
FRAMEWORK 
40. 
Tanzania launched the 
participatory 
PRS 
process 
in 
1999 
with 
the 
preparation 
of 
an 
interim 
PRS, 
followed 
by 
the 
approval 
of 
a 
full 
PRS 
in 
2000 
and the  presentation 
of 
the 
first 
PRS 
progress 
report 
in 
2001. 
Endorsement 
by 
the 
Bank 
and 
IMF 
Boards 
of 
the 
PRS 
and 
the 
first 
progress 
report 
enabled 
Tanzania 
to 
gain 
access 
to 
irrevocable 
debt 
relief 
by 
reaching 
the 
completion 
point 
under 
the 
enhanced 
HIPC Initiative 
in 
December 
200 
1. 
41. 
Poverty reduction 
has 
been 
a 
policy 
objective 
in 
Tanzania 
for 
four 
decades, 
but 
the 
growth 
strategy 
relied 
on 
excessive 
state 
interventions, 
and 
government  programs 
were 
typically 
underfunded  and 
poorly 
prioritized. 
The 
PRS 
represents 
a 
significant 
departure 
from 
past 
approaches 
to 
poverty 
reduction 
in 
several 
respects. 
42. 
Policies 
for 
sustained 
economic 
growth 
are 
now 
assigned 
high 
priority, 
and the importance 
of 
maintaining 
macroeconomic 
stability 
is 
fully 
acknowledged. 
Moreover, 
the 
PRS 
lays 
out 
the 
remaining 
medium-term structural 
challenges 
and 
discusses measures 
and expenditure programs 
to 
address 
them 
in 
the 
framework 
of 
a 
prioritized 
medium-term 
action plan. 
The 
strategy 
has 
evolved through 
a broad-based 
participatory 
process. 
Listening 
to 
the 
poor, 
to 
civil 
society, 
nongovernmental  organizations 
(NGOs), 
and  various 
other 
stakeholders 
has 
enabled 
the 
government 
to 
recognize 
and 
address 
the 
multidimensional 
aspects 
of 
poverty. 
Given 
the fragmentary  statistical 
data 
on 
poverty, 
the 
need 
for 
further 
work 
on 
reform 
strategies 
in 
some 
key 
areas, 
and 
the 
uncertainty about 
future 
financing 
resources, 
the 
current  PRS 
is 
a 
work 
in 
progress 
that 
will 
be 
refined 
as 
new 
information 
becomes 
available. Tanzania 
has adopted a 
rolling 
approach 
to 
updating the 
PRS 
and 
its 
action 
plan, 
learning 
from 
implementation 
experience. 
0 
The 
PRS 
has 
also  become  an 
important 
instrument 
for 
coordination 
within 
the 
government 
to 
ensure 
coherence 
of 
the various 
sectoral and cross-sectoral 
reform 
efforts 
in 
support 
of 
poverty 
reduction. 
The 
Interministerial  Technical  Committee 
(IMTC), 
which 
is 
chaired 
by 
the 
chief 
secretary 
meets 
quarterly 
to 
review 
progress 
on 
the various 
reform 
efforts, 
and 
the 
president 
chairs 
the 
annual 
review 
of 
progress 
in 
implementing 
the 
action 
plans undertaken 
by 
government 
departments, 
regions, and 
districts. 
Finally, 
the 
PRS 
correctly 
recognizes 
that 
the 
struggle against 
poverty 
will 
require 
better 
donor 
coordination, 
improved 
integration 
of 
external 
assistance 
into 
the 
budget, 
and 
simplification 
of 
aid 
disbursement 
procedures. 
Implementation 
of 
the 
Tanzania 
Assistance Strategy 
(TAS)’ 
aimed 
at addressing 
these 
concerns 
is 
being 
monitored 
by 
the 
Independent 
Monitoring 
Group 
(IMG), 
whose 
report 
feeds 
into inclusive 
deliberations 
for 
further 
improvement. 
0 
0 
0 
The 
TAS 
is 
a 
government 
initiative 
aimed 
at 
restoring local 
leadership 
and 
ownership 
of 
Tanzania’s development 
agenda. 
It 
provides 
a 
coherent 
national 
development 
framework 
for 
managing external 
resources 
to 
achieve 
the 
development 
strategies 
as 
stated 
in 
the 
Vision 
2025, 
the 
NPES, 
and 
the 
PRSP. 
15 
Page  26
43. 
The 
PRS 
focuses 
on 
three 
main 
areas 
of 
outcomes and actions 
for 
realizing 
them. 
The 
first 
is 
reduction 
in 
the 
breadth and 
depth 
of 
income 
poverty. 
The 
outcome 
targets 
include reduction 
of 
basic needs 
poverty 
and 
food 
poverty, 
with 
a 
particular 
focus 
on 
rural 
areas, 
where 
poverty 
is 
most 
prevalent. 
The 
second 
area 
is 
that 
of 
improving 
the 
quality 
of 
life 
and social 
well-being. 
This 
entails 
improved 
human 
capabilities,  enhanced 
longevity 
and 
survival,  social 
inclusion 
and 
personal 
security, 
improved 
nutrition, 
and 
containment 
of 
extreme 
vulnerability 
(mainly 
through 
social 
safety 
nets). 
The 
third 
broad 
area 
concerns  sustaining an 
environment that 
is 
conducive 
to 
development, 
which 
encompasses 
macroeconomic 
stability 
and 
good 
governance. 
44. 
The 
first 
and 
third 
areas 
entail 
cross-sectoral and 
institutional 
measures, 
and 
the 
second 
is 
primarily 
dealt 
with 
through 
sector-specific 
interventions 
in 
areas 
such 
as 
health, education, 
and 
water, 
which 
are designated 
as 
priority 
sectors. 
As 
part 
of 
the 
PRS 
process, 
the 
government 
has 
prepared,  realigned, 
and  approved 
sector 
development 
programs 
for 
health,  basic  education, 
agriculture,  and 
water. 
These 
now 
provide 
the 
framework 
for 
supporting 
the 
implementation 
of 
the PRS 
targets 
that 
are 
specific 
to 
the 
sectors. 
45. 
The 
PRS 
is 
explicit 
with 
regard 
to 
targets 
for 
resource 
allocation 
to 
priority 
sectors, 
which 
are 
defined 
as 
(a) 
education (notably 
at 
the 
primary 
school 
level); 
(b) 
health (primary health 
care); 
(c) 
agriculture 
(research and extension); 
(d) 
roads 
in 
rural 
areas; 
(e) 
water; 
(0 
the 
justice 
system; 
(g) 
improved 
governance, 
including 
combating 
corruption; 
and 
(h) 
HIV/AIDS. 
The 
government 
intends 
to 
focus 
poverty 
reduction 
resource 
allocation on 
these 
priority 
areas 
and 
has 
made a 
deliberate 
decision 
to 
increase 
allocations 
for 
operations 
and 
maintenance expenditures, 
as 
well 
as 
remuneration 
to 
motivate 
personnel 
toward 
greater 
efficiency 
in 
the 
delivery 
of 
these 
services. 
These 
allocations 
are 
based 
on 
estimates 
of 
recurrent 
cost 
requirements  and 
several  sector- 
specific 
studies. 
The 
sector 
priorities 
and 
budget allocations 
need 
to 
be 
sustained over 
time to 
maximize 
the returns 
to 
poverty 
reduction, 
especially 
rural 
poverty 
reduction. 
46. 
Growth 
andpoverty 
reduction outlook: 
The 
PRS 
emphasizes 
that 
growth 
is 
a 
pillar 
of 
sustained 
elimination 
of 
poverty, 
which 
can 
only 
be facilitated 
by 
an 
improved 
environment 
for 
that 
growth. 
Achieving 
targets 
for 
the 
reduction 
of 
income 
poverty 
thus 
requires 
putting 
in 
place 
policies 
and 
institutions 
to 
support the achievement 
of 
higher 
and 
sustained 
growth. 
In 
addition, 
there 
are 
PRS 
targets 
for 
services such 
as 
health 
and education, 
which 
are 
aimed 
at 
improving 
social 
well-being 
and 
enhancing the capacity 
of 
the 
poor 
to 
exploit 
income-earning 
opportunities 
from 
growth. 
47. 
The 
structure 
of 
the 
Tanzanian 
economy 
is 
characterized 
by 
heavy 
reliance 
on 
agriculture, 
which 
contributes 
45 
percent 
of 
GDP 
and 
thus 
provides the 
backbone 
of 
sustained 
economic 
growth 
and 
poverty 
reduction. 
One 
of 
the 
PRSC’s 
areas 
of 
focus 
is 
therefore 
setting 
up 
a 
program 
aimed 
at 
fostering 
rural 
and 
agriculture 
sector 
development. 
The 
ASDP 
presents a comprehensive 
package 
of 
measures 
to 
foster 
sustained 
agricultural 
growth, 
and 
its 
approval 
and 
implementation 
are 
thus 
part 
of 
the performance 
criteria 
of 
the 
PRSC. 
Limited 
access 
to 
agricultural 
credit, 
high 
levels 
of 
agricultural 
taxation, 
insecurity 
with 
respect 
to 
property 
rights 
to 
land 
and 
its 
use 
as 
collateral 
for 
credit, 
as 
well 
as 
limited 
access 
to 
markets 
as 
a 
result 
of 
poor 
transport 
infrastructure,  have 
been 
identified 
as 
key 
constraints 
to 
agriculture 
sector 
growth 
and 
are 
thus 
targeted 
under 
the 
PRSC. 
These 
measures 
are 
expected 
to allow 
the 
agriculture 
sector 
to grow 
on 
a sustained basis 
by 
5 
percent 
annually. 
48. 
Although 
agriculture 
sector 
growth 
is 
critical 
to 
overall 
economic 
growth 
because 
of 
the 
importance 
of 
the 
sector 
to 
GDP, 
achieving 
the 
poverty  reduction 
targets 
will 
also 
require 
accelerated 
growth 
in 
the 
industrial 
and service 
sectors. 
The 
government 
has 
withdrawn 
from 
most economic 
activities, 
and 
the 
private 
sector 
is 
now 
unequivocally 
recognized 
as 
playing 
the 
16 
Page  27
lead 
role 
in 
these  sectors. 
As 
a 
result 
of 
the 
liberalization 
of 
the 
economy 
and  sustained 
macroeconomic  stability, 
these sectors 
have 
grown 
between 
5 
and 6 percent 
annually 
in 
recent 
years. 
However, 
high 
cost 
and 
limited 
availability 
of 
infrastructure 
services, 
human 
resource and 
labor market 
barriers, and 
red 
tape 
in 
the 
public 
sector are 
the 
principal 
constraints 
to 
even 
higher 
growth 
rates 
in 
these 
sectors. 
Although 
infrastructure bottlenecks 
and 
human 
resource 
constraints 
are 
being 
addressed 
through 
accelerated 
development 
of 
the 
transport 
and 
power 
sector, 
the PRSC 
focuses 
on 
further 
improvements 
in 
the 
business 
environment 
and 
PSD, 
with 
particular attention 
to 
SMEs. 
Implementation 
of 
these 
measures, 
which 
are 
leveraged 
by 
the 
PRSC, 
should 
allow 
a 
scaling 
up 
of 
growth 
in 
industry 
to 
between 
8 
and 
10 
percent 
per 
year 
and 
growth 
in 
the 
service 
sector 
between 
6 
and 
8 percent 
per 
year. Sustained 
strong performance 
in 
the 
mining 
sector and 
the 
tourisms 
sector, 
which 
have been 
important 
contributors 
to 
economic 
growth 
in 
recent 
years, 
will 
add 
to 
the 
overall 
positive 
outlook 
for 
sustained 
growth 
performance and 
poverty 
reduction.

Tanzania’s 
growth 
and 
poverty reduction 
targets 
are 
defined 
in 
the 
context 
of 
the 
PRS, 
which 
encompasses 
the 
MDGs. 
With 
respect 
to 
income poverty, 
the 
target 
is 
to 
halve income 
poverty 
by 
2010, 
that 
is, 
reduce 
income 
poverty 
from 
the current 36 
percent 
to 
18 
percent. 
Using 
estimates 
for 
poverty 
elasticity, 
which 
range 
for 
Tanzania 
between 
0.53 
and 
1.09, 
estimates 
in 
table 
2 
indicate 
annual 
real growth 
rates 
required 
to 
achieve 
various 
poverty reduction 
rates, 
assuming unchanged income 
distribution. 
To 
the 
extent 
that 
patterns 
of 
growth 
favor the 
poor, 
lower growth 
rates also 
could 
lead 
to 
the 
targeted 
poverty 
reduction. 
The 
latest 
CEM, 
published 
in 
2002, 
indicates that achieving 
such 
growth 
rates 
would 
require 
sustained 
improvements 
in 
the 
institutional 
and 
policy 
environment 
as 
well 
as 
significant 
investments 
in 
human 
and 
physical 
capital. 
The 
PRSC 
program 
supports 
these 
improvements.

Poverty 
by 
2015

-

poverty 
by 
2015 
in 
line 
with 
the 
MDG 
targets 
would 
require 
sustained 
annual 
per 
capita 
GDP 
growth 
of 
at least 
4.4 
percent. 
Reaching 
the 
target 
already 
by 
201 
0 
as- 
indicated 
in 
the

would 
require 
average 
annual 
per

GDP 
growth 
of 
6.8 
percent. 
The 
principal 
supporting 
measures 
required 
to 
achieve 
these 
growth 
targets 
are 
(a) 
maintaining 
sound 
macroeconomic 
policies 
and 
intensified implementation 
of 
market 
reforms aimed 
at 
bolstering 
market efficiency, notably 
in 
agriculture; 
(b) 
channeling 
limited 
government 
resources 
toward 
the 
support 
of 
key 
programs and social 
services 
and enhancing 
efficiency 
and 
transparency 
in 
their 
application; 
(c) 
increasing 
emphasis 
on 
reforms aimed 
at 
promoting 
export-oriented  expansion 
and 
diversification 
of 
“pro-poor” 
sectors; 
and 
(d) 
increasing 
investment 
as 
a percentage 
of 
GDP 
from 
15 
percent 
to 
approximately 
17 
percent.
