under 
the 
PER 
process. 
D. 
Benefits 
and 
Risks 
Benefits 
174. 
The 
PRSCl 
is 
designed 
to 
help 
the 
government 
implement 
its 
homegrown 
PRS 
and reach 
the goals 
articulated 
in 
the 
strategy. 
The reforms 
supported under the 
program 
will 
help 
raise 
growth 
and 
strengthen 
the 
institutional 
infrastructure 
needed 
to 
improve 
the 
public 
sector’s 
capacity 
to 
carry out 
its 
role 
more 
effectively, 
as 
envisioned 
in 
the PRS. 
A 
stable macroeconomic 
environment, 
improved 
budget 
execution, 
strong 
economic 
governance, 
an 
environment 
conducive 
to 
private 
sector and 
rural 
development (especially 
enhanced 
agricultural 
productivity 
and 
profitability), 
and 
a 
well-performing public 
sector 
offering tlie 
improvcd delivery 
of 
public 
services 
are 
essential 
if 
the government 
is 
to 
reach 
its 
goals 
for 
poverty 
reduction 
and 
improve 
the 
58 
Page  69
quality 
of 
life 
in 
Tanzania. 
A 
vital 
related 
element 
would 
be 
the 
effective 
monitoring 
and 
assessment 
of 
the 
progress 
and 
pitfalls 
related 
to 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
PRS. 
Risks 
and 
Efforts 
to 
Mitigate 
Them 
175. 
Because 
the 
proposed 
PRSCl 
supports 
the 
implementation 
of 
Tanzania’s 
PRS, 
there 
are 
two 
closely 
interrelated  and overlapping 
sets 
of 
risks. 
The 
first 
set 
covers 
all 
risks 
that 
may 
interfere 
with 
the 
achievement 
of 
the 
objectives 
of 
the 
PRS. 
The 
second 
is 
narrower 
and concerns 
risks 
to 
the 
successful 
implementation 
of 
actions 
covered 
in 
the 
PRSC 
policy 
matrix. 
The 
following 
risks 
can be 
identified 
at 
this 
stage: 
0 
As 
the 
next round 
of 
elections 
approaches, 
concerns about 
the 
durability 
of 
commitment 
to 
reforms 
will 
mount. 
To 
mitigate 
this 
risk, 
it 
will 
be 
essential 
to 
implement  legal 
and 
institutional 
changes 
to 
underpin reforms 
by 
the current government. 
This 
will 
reduce 
the 
risk 
of 
policy 
reversals and 
root 
these 
reforms more 
deeply 
in 
both 
attitude 
and 
practice. 
A 
program (ATIP) 
to 
support 
the 
government’s 
effort 
in 
bringing 
the 
institutional 
and 
legal 
framework 
in 
line 
with 
the achievement 
to 
date 
is 
under way. 
Inspiring 
investors’ 
belief 
that the 
changes 
made 
are 
durable 
is 
a 
major 
challenge 
in 
scaling 
up 
investor 
response. 
An 
important 
means 
to 
sustain 
reform 
and 
limit 
the 
possibility 
of 
policy 
reversals 
is 
the 
ongoing 
transition 
from 
a 
conditionality-based 
approach 
to 
reforms,. 
to 
the 
building 
of 
domestic 
constituencies 
for 
reform-which 
provides 
a 
more 
durable 
and 
secure 
basis 
for 
ensuring 
the sustainability 
of 
reform 
efforts. 
As 
policymaking 
has 
become 
more 
inclusive-with 
a greater 
role 
for 
stakeholders 
beyond 
the 
executive 
and, 
in 
particular, 
for 
parliament4ecisionmaking 
processes 
have 
tended 
to 
become 
more 
drawn 
out. 
This 
poses 
a 
risk 
primarily 
with 
respect 
to 
the 
speed 
of 
implementation 
of 
agreed 
actions, 
while 
the 
risk 
for 
significant 
policy 
reversals 
is 
considered 
to 
be 
reduced. 
The 
key 
mitigating 
measures 
contain 
two 
elements. 
The 
first 
is 
dissemination 
and knowledge sharing 
with 
a 
wide 
range 
of 
domestic constituencies 
on 
the 
rationale 
and benefits 
of 
reforms. 
The 
second relates 
to 
using 
the 
process 
leading 
to 
the 
definition 
of 
the 
PRSC 
policy 
matrix 
(PAF) 
to 
gauge 
the 
extent 
to 
which 
there 
is 
consensus 
on 
reforms 
among 
key 
constituencies and 
then 
sequence 
reforms accordingly. 
0 
Another 
risk 
for 
the 
achievement 
of 
PRS 
objectives 
arises 
from 
the 
ongoing 
decentralization 
process, 
which 
shifts 
responsibility 
for 
key 
poverty reducing 
programs 
in 
education, health, 
the water 
sector, 
and 
agriculture 
to 
the districts. 
Preliminary 
assessments 
indicate 
great 
variations 
in 
the 
quality 
and capacity 
of 
financial 
management 
and 
reporting 
systems 
across 
districts, 
in 
addition 
to 
often 
limited 
capacities 
to 
manage 
and 
implement 
poverty 
reducing 
programs. 
The 
local 
government 
reform 
program 
focuses 
on 
strengthening 
the 
capacity 
of 
local 
authorities. However, 
a 
residual 
risk 
remains, 
especially 
in 
the 
initial 
phases 
of 
the 
PRSC, 
that capacity-building efforts 
may 
lag 
behind 
the 
scaling 
up 
of 
programs. 
All 
phases 
of 
the 
PRSC 
support 
actions 
to 
strengthen 
the 
watchdog 
role 
of 
beneficiaries 
at 
the 
community 
level 
through 
disclosure 
of 
the 
flow 
of 
resources 
to 
various 
activities 
at 
the 
grassroots 
level 
and 
expenditure 
tracking 
of 
financial 
flows 
from 
the 
central 
government 
through 
the 
annual 
PERs. 
These 
actions 
will 
help 
to 
assess 
and 
mitigate 
risk. 
Although 
systems 
and 
capacities at 
the 
central 
level 
are 
generally 
stronger 
than 
at 
the 
local 
level, 
limited 
human 
resources 
capacity 
to 
implement 
and manage 
the 
PRSC 
reform 
agenda 
at the 
center represents another 
risk 
in 
the Tanzanian 
context, 
especially 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
public 
expenditure 
management,  procurement,  and 
financial 
management. 
To 
mitigate 
these 
risks, 
the 
proposed PRSC focuses 
appropriately 
on 
the 
strengthening 
of 
oversight 
arrangements. 
A 
large 
number 
of 
capacity-building  efforts 
are 
in 
place. 
Most 
reform 
programs have 
capacity-building 
components 
embedded, 
including 
the 
PFMRP, 
the 
local 
0 
e 
59 
Page  70
government 
reform 
program, 
and 
the 
public 
sector 
reform 
programs. 
Similarly, 
sector 
programs 
and 
projects 
typically 
also 
contain 
capacity 
building 
components. 
The 
challenge 
for 
Tanzania 
is 
to 
enhance 
the 
effectiveness 
of 
capacity-building efforts 
by 
shifting 
from 
supply-driven 
to 
demand-driven 
efforts 
and 
address 
capacity 
building 
in 
a 
holistic 
way, 
in 
which 
the 
incentive 
structure, 
education 
system, 
and 
specific 
capacity-building efforts 
are 
organized 
in 
a 
mutually  reinforcing 
manner. 
Analytic 
work 
to 
address 
these 
issues 
is 
planned. 
In 
the 
medium 
term, 
it 
is 
expected 
that 
the 
shift 
from 
project 
to 
budget 
support 
by 
the 
Bank 
and  other 
donors 
will 
release 
staff 
who 
are 
currently 
tied 
up 
in 
project 
management 
to 
play 
a 
greater 
role 
in 
overall public 
sector 
management. 
To 
ensure 
that 
such 
risks 
do 
not 
derail 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
PRS  and 
PRSC, 
appropriate 
monitoring 
instruments 
have 
been 
put 
in 
place. 
The 
government’s  annual PRS 
progress report, 
which 
builds 
on 
the 
recently 
developed, 
comprehensive 
poverty 
monitoring 
system, 
and 
a 
well-established  annual 
PER 
mechanism, 
provide 
open  and 
transparent 
arrangements 
for 
monitoring 
outcomes 
on 
fiduciary 
risks. 
Moreover, 
the 
annual nature 
of 
the PRSC provides 
a 
framework 
for 
appraising 
risks 
identified 
in 
the 
CFAA 
and 
CPAR 
and 
agreeing 
on 
necessary 
corrective 
actions.

I 
am 
writing 
to 
request, 
on 
behalf 
of 
the 
Government 
of 
the 
United 
Republic 
of 
Tanzania, 
the 
first 
Poverty 
Reduction 
Support 
Credit 
and 
Grant 
(PRSCI) 
of  US$ 
132 
million 
(SDR 
equivalent) 
of 
which 
US$32 
million 
will 
be 
grant in support 
of 
our Poverty 
Reduction 
Strategy. 
The 
proposed credit 
will 
reinforce our

to 
reduce

through 
maintaining

stability, enhancing 
pro- 
poor

process,

will 
help 
to

needs 
in

priority sectors 
which 
have a

identifiable link 
to

as  defined 
in 
the

Reduction 
Strategy 
Progress Reports. 
1. 
BACKGROUND 
AND 
RECENT 
DEVELOPMENTS 
A. 
MACROECONOMIC 
PERFORMANCE 
2. 
Tanzania's 
macroeconomic performance 
continued 
to improve 
in 
2001/02. 
The 
GDP 
growth rate increased 
to 
5.6 
percent 
in 
2001 
and 
is 
estimated 
to 
have risen 
by 
5.9 
percent 
in 
2002 
despite 
a 
substantial deterioration 
in 
the 
terms 
of 
trade and 
a 
slow 
down 
in 
62 
Page  73
tourism-related 
travel 
due 
to 
the  adverse global 
security 
situation. 
Growth 
in 
2001 
stemmed  from 
relatively 
strong  performances 
in 
agriculture, 
mining, 
wholesale 
and 
retail 
trade, 
as 
well 
as 
manufacturing. 
The 
agriculture 
sector 
grew 
by 
5.5 
percent 
in 
2001 
up 
from 
3.4 percent 
in 
2000, with 
most 
of 
the 
growth 
emanating 
from 
food 
crop 
production 
and fishing. 
The 
growth 
of 
the 
mining 
sector 
remained 
strong 
at 
13.2  percent  and 
its 
contribution 
to 
GDP 
increased 
to 
2.5 
percent. 
Wholesale 
and 
retail  trade 
(including 
tourism) 
rose 
by 
7 
percent 
in 
2001. 
Industrial 
output 
rose 
by 
6 
percent. 
Growth 
in 
2002103 
is 
expected 
to 
stem from 
relatively 
strong 
performances 
in 
these 
same 
areas. 
The 
12-month rate of 
inflation 
declined 
further, 
from 
5.5 
percent 
in 
December 
2000 
to 
4.4 
percent 
in 
January 2003. 
Gross 
official reserves 
of 
the 
Bank 
of 
Tanzania 
(BOT) 
stayed above 
five 
months 
of 
imports 
of 
goods 
and services. 
B. 
TANZANIA 
POVERTY 
REDUCTION 
STRATEGY 
AND 
IMPLEMENTATION 
3. 
Tanzania 
remains 
committed 
to the goal 
of 
eradicating 
abject 
poverty 
set 
out 
in 
the' 
Development 
Vision 
2025. 
The framework 
for 
achieving 
this 
goal 
is 
articulated 
in 
the 
National 
Poverty 
Eradication 
Strategy 
(NPES 
- 
1997), 
the 
Poverty  Reduction  Strategy 
Paper 
(PRSP 
2000), 
and 
the 
Zanzibar 
Poverty Reduction 
Plan 
(2002). 
As 
at 
present, 
in 
the 
medium 
term 
the 
Government 
will 
continually 
seek 
to 
achieve 
the 
targets 
and 
outcomes specified 
in 
the annual 
PRS 
progress 
reports, 
and 
will 
maintain  policy 
intewentions, 
including 
budgetary 
allocations 
that 
reflect 
these 
priorities. 
Central 
to 
the 
PRS 
are 
the maintenance 
of 
macroeconomic stability, acceleration 
of 
pro- 
poor 
growth, 
and 
creation 
of 
employment. 
4. 
More 
specifically, 
the 
government's 
medium-term 
pro-poor 
growth 
strategy 
lies in 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
and 
rural 
development 
programmes, 
and further 
strengthening of 
the 
investment 
climate to 
attract 
private capital 
and 
technology, 
in 
order 
to 
lift 
productivity 
in 
agriculture and 
industry, 
particularly 
through small 
and 
medium-size 
enterprises. In 
this 
connection, 
the government 
will 
seek to 
achieve 
sustainable 
improvement 
in 
infrastructure, 
notably 
in 
transport, 
communications 
and 
energy. 
At 
the 
same 
time 
we 
are 
committed 
to 
removing 
remaining  weaknesses 
in 
the  regulatory 
63 
Page  74
framework,  law 
and order, tax 
administration, corruption 
and public 
administration. 
C. 
DEVELOPMENTS 
IN 
BUDGET 
SUPPORT 
AS 
THE 
MAIN 
INSTRUMENT 
FOR 
AID 
DISBURSEMENT 
IN 
SUPPORT 
OF 
THE 
PRS 
5. 
In 
1997 the 
Government 
requested 
the 
donor 
community  to 
provide assistance 
for 
the 
establishment 
of 
a 
Multilateral 
Debt 
Relief 
Fund 
(MDF). 
The 
intention 
of 
the 
fund 
was 
to 
provide 
support 
from 
donors 
towards 
servicing 
debt  obligations 
to 
multilateral  financial 
institutions. 
Disbursement 
of 
funds 
was 
based 
on 
actual 
debt 
obligations 
that 
matured 
during  a 
specific  period. 
In 
turn, 
the 
Government 
committed 
to 
allocate 
the 
budget 
savings 
from 
the 
MDF 
scheme 
to 
protected 
priority 
items 
in 
the 
social 
sectors. Countries 
contributing 
to 
the 
MDF 
were 
Denmark, 
Finland, 
Ireland, 
the 
Netherlands, 
Norway, 
Sweden, 
Switzerland 
and 
the 
United Kingdom. 
6. 
In 
November 
2001, 
Tanzania  reached 
decision 
point 
under 
enhanced 
Highly 
Indebted 
Poor Countries 
(HIPC) 
initiative. 
In 
order 
to accommodate 
this 
change, 
at 
the 
start 
of 
the 
2001102 
fiscal year 
all 
MDF 
donors 
transferred their disbursements to 
the 
Poverty Reduction 
Budget 
Support 
(PRBS) 
facility. 
Additional  members 
joining 
the 
PRBS facility 
were 
the 
European 
Union 
in 
fiscal year 
2000101, 
Japan 
in 
2001102 
and 
Canada 
in 
2002103, 
giving a total 
of 
eleven 
PRBS 
donors. 
7. 
A 
joint 
PRBS  Annual 
Review/ 
PRSC 
pre-appraisal 
was 
undertaken 
in 
November 
2002 
to 
harmonise 
the 
PRBS 
facility with 
the 
World 
Bank's 
PRSC 
(which 
replaces 
it's 
Programmatic Structural 
Adjustment Credit, 
PSAC 
from FY 
2003/04). 
This 
move 
to 
a 
common 
framework 
for 
poverty-oriented budget 
support 
from all sources will 
help 
to 
reduce 
overall transactions 
costs 
(through 
the agreement 
of 
a 
common 
policy 
Performance 
Assessment 
Framework 
(PAF) 
matrix 
which 
is 
to  be 
reviewed 
jointly 
by 
all 
stakeholders), 
as 
well 
as 
to 
reduce 
overall 
fiscal 
risk 
for 
the 
Government 
and 
the 
economy 
by 
raising predictability 
of 
inflows. 
From 
2003104 
the 
Government and 
the 
donors 
will 
consider 
the 
Partnership 
Framework 
(PF) 
- 
the 
harmonised 
PRBS 
and 
PRSC  budget 
support 
framework 
- 
as 
the 
common 
instrument 
of 
support 
to 
the 
Poverty 
Reduction  Strategy 
64 
Page  75
(PRS) 
through 
the Government's 
budget. 
The 
PF 
will 
be 
an integral 
part of 
the 
Government of 
Tanzania's 
public 
sector 
budgeting and 
priority setting 
process 
and 
the PRS. 
The 
budget 
will continue to 
be 
used 
as a tool to 
reach 
the 
PRS 
objectives, 
and 
priority 
sectors 
as 
defined 
in 
the 
PRS 
will 
be protected in 
the budget. 
8. 
The 
Partnership Framework 
Memorandum 
drawn 
up 
during 
the 
joint 
PRBS 
Annual 
ReviewlPRSC 
1 
pre-appraisal 
process 
in 
November 
2002 describes 
the 
principles 
of 
the common framework 
for 
the 
development  grant 
and  loan 
agreements 
to 
fund 
poverty- 
oriented 
budget 
support 
to 
Tanzania. 
11. 
THE 
MEDIUM 
TERM 
REFORM 
PROGRAMME 
FOR 
POVERTY 
REDUCTION 
A. 
IMPROVED 
INVESTMENT 
AND BUSINESS 
ENVIRONMENT 
9. 
Given the 
importance 
of 
an 
effective 
public/private partnership 
in 
achieving 
better 
policies 
and 
a 
more 
enabling 
business 
environment for 
private 
sector 
development (that 
is, a better policy, 
administrative,  legal 
and 
regulatory 
environment), 
the 
Government 
approved 
the 
Business 
Environment 
Strengthening 
in 
Tanzania 
(BEST) 
programme 
on 
July 
22nd 
2002. 
The 
programme  aims 
at 
contributing 
to 
poverty 
reduction through 
private-sector-led 
economic 
growth 
and 
activities 
focusing 
on 
small 
and 
medium-sized 
enterprises. 
The 
programme 
recognises the 
key 
importance 
of 
an 
effective public/private partnership 
in 
achieving 
better 
policies and 
a 
more 
enabling 
business 
environment. 
As 
such, 
during 
the 
PRSC 
I 
pre-appraisal, 
the 
Government 
committed 
to  the  following 
PRSCI 
prior 
action: 
Approval 
of 
implementation 
plan 
for 
the 
Business 
Environment Strengthening 
of 
Tanzania 
(BEST) 
program 
by 
the 
Integrated Framework 
Steering 
Committee. 
Approval 
was 
made 
in 
February 
2003 
for the implementation 
of the 
BEST 
programme. 
In 
order 
to 
support the 
implementation 
of 
the 
programme, 
the 
Government 
is 
committed 
to 
the establishment 
and 
staffing 
of 
a 
Better 
Regulation Unit 
in 
the President's Office-Planning 
and Privatisation, 
with 
subsequent reviews conducted 
to monitor 
the 
implementation 
of 
BEST. 
65 
Page  76
IO. 
The government 
continues with 
its 
commitment to 
withdraw 
from 
economic activities 
and 
to 
create 
an 
enabling 
environment for 
private 
sector 
investment. 
The 
parastatal 
reform 
programme 
continues to 
make 
progress 
with 
a 
focus 
now on 
privatization 
of 
large 
utilities, companies 
and 
banks. 
Recent 
developments 
include 
the 
privatization 
of 
Air 
Tanzania Corporation (ATC), 
the 
concessioning 
of 
Dar es Salaam Water 
and 
Sewerage Authority 
(DAWASA), 
and 
the 
reaching 
of 
an  advanced 
stage 
of 
privatization 
of 
the  Tanzania 
Railways Corporation (TRC), the 
Tanzania 
Harbours 
Authority 
(THA) 
and the National Micro-finance 
Bank 
(NMB). 
In 
addition, the process 
of 
unbundling 
the 
Tanzania 
Electric 
Supply 
Company  (TANESCO) 
into 
autonomous commercial 
entities 
before privatisation 
is 
ongoing. 
A private management 
company, which 
took over 
in 
April 
2002, 
has 
begun  to  improve 
the 
finances 
of 
TANESCO. 
The 
government 
budget 
for 
2002/03  has made 
adequate 
provisions for 
payment 
of 
retrenchment benefits to 
employees 
who 
may 
be 
retrenched 
in 
the 
process 
of 
restructuring 
or 
privatisation 
of 
these public entities. 
11. 
In line with the 
PRSP, the 
Government 
aims 
to 
adopt 
a private 
sector development strategy 
-, 
with 
an 
aim 
of 
securing approval for 
its 
implementation by March 
2005. 
The President’s Office, Planning and 
Privatisation 
is 
committed to 
producing 
the strategy 
by 
January 2004 
through integrated public and 
private sector 
dialogue. 
12. 
The  Government 
is 
committed 
to 
the  Small 
and 
Medium 
Enterprises 
(SME) 
development 
policy, 
which 
was 
adopted 
in 
February 
2003 
and 
is, expected 
to be 
ready 
for  implementation 
by 
November 2003. 
It 
has been 
recognised 
that 
SMEs 
play 
a crucial 
role 
in 
employment  creation 
and 
income 
generation 
in 
Tanzania. 
Strategies 
for 
implementing  the 
SME 
development  policy 
in  the 
medium 
term focus 
on 
three main 
areas, 
namely 
the 
creation 
of 
an 
enabling 
business  environment; 
the 
development 
of  financial 
and 
non-financial  services 
strategies; 
and 
the 
establishment 
of 
a 
supportive institutional infrastructure. 
It 
takes 
into 
account 
the 
specific 
constraints 
faced 
by 
this 
sector 
and 
aims 
at 
strengthening institutions 
that 
will address 
these 
constraints. 
GOT 
will promote consultations 
and  partnerships 
with 
the 
private 
sector 
to 
ensure  appropriate 
representation of 
SME 
relevant issues 
on 
the 
agenda 
of 
Tanzania 
National  Business  Council. 
Furthermore 
Ministry 
of 
Industry  and 
66 
Page  77
Trade 
(MolT) 
will 
have 
the 
overall responsibility 
for 
coordinating 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
SME 
Development 
Policy. 
MolT 
will  also 
coordinate 
the 
activities 
of 
all 
SME 
stakeholders 
and 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
organizing, 
monitoring, 
and 
evaluation 
of 
implementation 
of 
the 
SME 
Development 
Policy. 
13. 
In 
order 
to  establish 
clear  property rights that 
facilitate 
the 
securitisation 
of 
land, 
the 
mortgage 
industry 
and 
streamlined 
procedures for 
real 
estate 
transactions, 
the Government 
recognises 
that a 
key 
issue 
is 
the 
ability 
of 
individuals to 
use 
land 
as 
collateral. 
In light 
of 
this, 
the 
Government 
is 
considering 
amending 
the Land 
Act 
of 
1999. 
If 
deemed 
necessary, 
the 
amendment 
to 
the Land Act 
would 
be 
submitted 
to 
Parliament 
before 
the 
end 
of 
2003. 
Other 
policy 
measures 
in 
this 
area 
will 
aim 
at 
improving 
the 
land registry, 
and 
enhancing 
the 
capacity 
of 
the  commercial 
court, 
including 
the 
employment 
and 
training 
of 
more judges, 
with 
a 
view 
to 
keeping 
the 
average 
case 
resolution period 
below 
six 
months. 
The 
Government 
is 
also 
considering 
other avenues 
for promoting property 
rights. 
B. 
AGRICULTURAL 
AND 
RURAL 
DEVELOPMENT 
14. 
As 
a 
lead 
sector 
of 
the 
economy 
and 
a 
key 
to progress 
in 
social 
development 
of 
the 
population 
in 
rural 
areas, 
the 
Government 
sees 
reform 
in 
agriculture 
as 
a 
high priority. 
The 
objectives 
in 
the 
medium 
term 
are to 
ensure 
food 
security;  improve 
nutritional 
and 
living standards; 
increase 
agricultural 
exports 
and 
outputs 
from agro- 
based  industries; 
promote 
integrated 
and 
sustainable 
use 
and 
management  of 
natural 
resources 
to 
conserve 
the 
environment; 
develop 
human 
resources; 
improve 
animal  health 
and 
livestock 
productivity 
in 
order 
to 
enhance 
the 
quality 
of 
livestock 
products 
and 
income 
levels 
of 
farmers 
and 
livestock 
keepers. 
15. 
As 
a 
key 
part 
of 
the implementation 
of 
the 
PRS, 
the Agricultural 
Sector 
Development 
Strategy 
(ASDS) 
was 
completed and 
adopted 
in 
2001. 
ASDS 
identifies five  strategic 
issues 
for 
agriculture 
sector 
development: 
(i) 
strengthening 
the 
institutional 
framework; 
*(ii) 
creating 
a 
favourable  environment  for 
commercial 
activities; 
(iii) 
clarifying 
public 
and 
private 
sector roles in improving services; 
(iv) 
strengthening  marketing efficiency for 
inputs 
and 
outputs; 
and 
(v) 
mainstreaming planning 
for 
agricultural 
development 
in 
other 
sectors. 
67 
Page  78
16. 
The 
Agricultural 
Sector 
Development 
Programme 
(ASDP) 
is 
to 
be 
used 
as 
a 
tool 
for 
implementing 
the 
Agricultural 
Sector 
Development 
Strategy 
(ASDS). 
During 
the  PRSC 
1 
pre-appraisal, 
the 
Government 
committed 
to the 
following 
PRSC 
1 
prior 
action: 
Finalized 
and 
adopted 
for 
implementation 
the Agricultural 
Sector 
Development 
Programme 
(ASDP) 
Framework 
and 
Process 
document. 
The 
ASDP 
framework  and 
process 
document 
was 
completed 
in 
December 
2002. 
It  has 
been 
organized  around 
three 
sub- 
programmes, 
namely: 
agricultural sector 
support 
and 
implementation 
at 
district 
and field 
level 
through 
District 
Agricultural 
Development 
Programmes 
(DADPs) 
and 
District 
Development 
Plans 
(DDPs); 
agricultural 
sector 
support 
at 
the 
national level with a 
focus on the 
role 
of 
central 
government; 
and the 
management of 
ASDP 
links 
with 
other 
sectors. 
In 
the 
medium 
term 
the 
Government 
intends  to 
develop 
a 
monitoring 
system 
for 
the 
sector, 
which 
will 
set 
performance 
targets 
and 
indicators for 
tracking 
progress  made 
in 
implementing 
ASDP 
within 
the 
PRS 
monitoring 
framework. 
17. 
In 
order to 
increase 
agricultural 
trade 
in 
the 
medium term, the 
Government 
will 
review 
the role and 
funding 
arrangements 
of 
Crop 
Boards 
in 
order to limit their 
functions, 
which 
currently 
span services, 
taxation 
and 
the 
marketing 
of 
crops 
which 
they 
regulate. 
In 
order 
to 
improve internal 
trade, the 
Government 
has 
instructed 
all 
regions and 
district  actors  to 
remove 
road 
blocks 
which 
has 
facilitated  freer 
movements 
of 
agricultural 
products. 
There 
are 
also 
aims 
of 
developing 
and 
implementing 
a 
program 
to 
strengthen 
the 
institutional 
arrangements 
for 
district 
road 
maintenance 
and 
rehabilitation. 
In 
addition, 
in 
order  to 
promote 
the 
exportation 
of 
agricultural 
produce 
and 
increase 
efforts 
towards 
raising 
export 
earnings, there 
are on-going 
efforts to 
eliminate 
all 
non-tariff barriers 
within 
the 
EAC 
and 
SADC 
as well 
as 
to 
enhance 
performance 
of 
the 
recently 
established 
Export 
Credit 
Guarantee Scheme, 
which caters 
for 
traditional 
exports. 
68 
Page  79
18. 
In 
an 
attempt 
to

half of 
2002103.

Improved tenure 
security

and 
the 
Village Land Act. 
C. 
IMPROVED 
POVERTY 
MONITORING 
AND 
EVALUATION 
20. 
The 
annual 
PRS 
progress 
reports, 
which 
outline 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
PRS, 
continue 
to 
be 
formulated, implemented 
and monitored with effective 
participation 
of 
all 
stakeholders. 
The 
second  annual 
PRS 
progress 
report 
is 
due  to 
be 
finalized 
and 
published 
by 
the 
end 
of  March 
2003. 
Two 
agreed

and

consistent

of import of

i) 
Fiscal

26. 
The 
prevailing 
low

GDP 
ratio 
calls

ability

The

2003104

to 
raise 
the 
revenue

measured 
by

of

above 12.8 
percent. 
The 
policy

will 
focus towards 
curbing tax 
evasion, 
controlling 
tax 
exemptions 
and 
improving 
tax 
administration. 
27.

Broad guidelines

2002, 
Currently the

for 
the

i)

46. 
effective

An

PRSC 
1 
prior

This

2003. 
The 
overall system 
for 
public  management  is 
designed to 
ensure that  there 
is 
amongst 
others, 
minimization 
of 
resource leakages. 
Towards 
this 
end,

Government 
has 
put 
in

System  (IFMS). 
Under 
IFMS 
all 
Government 
Payments 
are 
centralized 
and 
payable 
at 
one 
single 
Central 
Payment 
Office. 
For

Sub 
Treasury 
. 
The

committed

of 
an 
Approval 
by

of

which

key

47. 
This

allows 
for 
maximum  monitoring  of 
all

initiated 
by 
MDAs 
at 
any 
given time.  Further, 
the 
IFMS 
has 
an 
in-built audit 
trail 
facility 
which 
enables tracking 
of 
all users 
of 
the 
system. 
This 
acts 
as 
a 
deterrent 
to 
would 
-be 
resource 
misappropriators. 
In 
addition, 
the 
system 
has 
a 
facility 
that controls 
payments  according 
to 
commitments  already 
entered,  commonly 
known 
as 
the

control. 
Under 
this 
facility 
an 
MDA 
cannot 
use resources 
that 
are 
already 
committed. 
Also, 
an 
MDA 
cannot 
effect 
payment 
in 
excess 
of 
a commitment.  Funds 
issued 
to 
MOAS

H. 
ENVIRONMENTAL 
SUSTAINABILITY 
52. 
The 
underlying 
thrust 
of 
the 
objectives 
as 
laid 
out 
in 
the 
Vision 
2025 
(1999) 
is 
to 
attain 
sustainable 
development 
of 
the 
country's 
people. 
The 
Government 
therefore 
recognizes 
that  there  are 
important 
implications 
for 
environmental 
policy. 
53. 
The 
Rural 
Development 
Strategy  (RDS) 
(2001) 
gives 
a 
very 
high 
priority 
to 
poverty reduction 
issues, 
and 
emphasizes 
that rural 
poverty has a major impact on the 
sustainable 
utilization 
of 
natural 
resources.  Natural 
resource 
management 
is 
identified 
as 
a strategic 
priority area, with 
the 
development 
objective 
of 
ensuring 
sustainable 
livelihoods 
and utilization of natural 
resources for 
the benefit 
of 
the 
rural and 
urban 
population. 
Similarly, 
pro-poor 
tourism development, 
sustained 
management 
and 
utilization 
of 
fish 
and 
forest resources 
for 
the  benefit 
of 
the 
majority 
of 
the 
rural 
and 
urban 
people, 
and 
the 
exploitation 
of 
wildlife 
to contribute to 
the 
welfare 
of 
the 
surrounding 
communities are 
all 
priority activities 
for 
the 
RDS, 
thereby 
ensuring 
that linkages 
are 
made 
between 
poverty reduction 
and 
environmental 
policies. 
In 
addition, 
the 
ASDP 
(2002) 
also 
acknowledges  the 
importance 
of 
sustainable 
natural 
resource 
management 
and 
environmental management 
is 
identified 
as 
an 
important 
cross-cutting 
issue 
at 
both national 
and 
local 
government levels. 
54.

Government 
has

CONCLUSION 
56. 
The 
Government is committed 
to the 
goal 
of 
reducing 
poverty 
as 
encompassed 
by 
the 
overall Poverty 
Reduction Strategy 
and 
the 
agreed 
policy 
actions 
laid  out 
in 
the 
PRBSIPRSC 
Performance 
Assessment 
Framework. 
However, 
the 
total 
resources 
available 
to 
Government 
are 
insufficient 
to 
implement poverty-reducing 
policies. 
While 
efforts 
are 
in 
place 
to raise 
the 
domestic 
revenue 
to 
GDP 
ratio, 
time 
is 
needed 
firstly 
to 
expand 
the 
revenue 
base 
from 
a 
higher 
growth 
rate 
of 
the 
economy 
and 
secondly 
to 
observe 
tangible results 
from 
those policy 
actions 
implemented 
to 
raise 
revenue. 
In 
light 
of 
these factors, 
the 
Government 
views 
the 
support 
from 
IDA 
through 
the 
proposed 
PRSCI, 
as 
a 
crucial 
part 
of 
your 
institution’s 
support 
in 
the 
effort 
to 
reduce 
poverty. 
Yours 
sincerely, 
mba 
(MP)

1. 
Main 
principles 
1) 
The 
Government 
and 
the 
donors consider the Partnership 
Framework22 
(PF) 
for 
budget 
suppod3 
as 
the 
common 
instrument 
of 
support 
to 
the 
Poverty Reduction 
Strategy 
(PRS) 
through 
the 
Government’s 
budget. 
The 
PF 
is 
an 
integral part 
of 
the 
Government 
of 
Tanzania’s 
public 
sector 
budgeting and 
priority 
setting 
process 
and 
the 
PRS. 
The 
budget 
will 
be 
used 
as 
a 
tool to 
reach 
the 
PRS objectives, 
and 
priority 
sectors 
as 
defined 
in 
the 
PRS 
will 
be 
protected 
in 
the 
budget. 
The 
agreed 
priority 
allocations 
as 
well 
as 
indicators 
of 
achievements 
will 
continue 
to 
be 
monitored 
through 
joint 
processes 
including 
the 
Public 
Expenditure 
Review 
(PER) and PRS 
process 
and 
reviews 
as 
detailed 
in 
this 
PF. 
These 
joint 
processes 
will 
involve 
the 
Government 
and 
the 
PF 
donors. 
2) 
This 
PF 
Memorandum 
describes 
the 
common 
framework 
for 
the 
development 
grant  and 
loan 
agreements 
to 
fund 
poverty-oriented 
programme 
budget 
support 
to 
Tanzania. 
Together 
with 
the 
attached 
Technical 
Annexes, 
it 
replaces 
the 
Memorandum 
of 
Understanding 
for 
the 
Poverty 
Reduction 
Budget 
Support 
(PRBS) 
signed 
on 
27‘” 
November 
2001 
and 
the 
Technical 
Note 
of 
October 
2001. 
This 
Performance 
Framework 
Memorandum 
has 
been 
reviewed 
and 
agreed 
by 
the 
Government and 
the 
PF 
donors 
during 
the 
joint 
Annual 
Review/pre-appraisal 
of 
general 
budget 
support 
carried out 
in 
November 
2002. 
3) 
The 
PF 
Memorandum 
will 
be 
supplemented 
by 
bilateral 
agreements 
between 
each 
individual 
donor 
and 
the 
Government 
of 
Tanzania, 
and 
by 
a 
PRSC 
programme document 
and 
related legal 
documents 
of 
the 
World 
Bank. 
The 
bilateral 
agreements 
will 
have 
the 
choice 
to 
focus 
on 
all 
or 
a subset 
of 
the 
actions 
and 
targets 
in 
the 
list 
of 
policy 
actions 
and targets 
jointly 
agreed 
on 
in 
the 
PAF. 
It 
is 
understood 
by 
the 
parties 
that 
the 
contents 
in 
the 
individual 
funding 
agreements take 
legal 
precedence 
over 
this 
Partnership 
Framework 
Memorandum. 
However, 
it 
is 
the 
intention 
of 
the 
parties 
to 
establish 
funding 
agreements 
that 
are 
compatible 
with 
the 
provisions 
of 
this 
Partnership 
Framework Memorandum. 
4) 
The 
intention 
is 
to 
manage 
existing 
and 
future 
budgetary 
support 
from 
donors  and 
financing 
institutions through 
the 
PF 
facility. 
The 
objectives 
are 
to 
minimize 
transaction 
costs, 
to 
harmonize 
performance 
benchmarks  and 
dialogue 
between 
the 
parties 
and 
to 
link 
funding 
commitments 
by 
the 
donors 
to 
achievement 
of 
targets 
set. 
The 
Partnership 
Framework 
and 
the 
funding 
Facility 
is 
therefore 
open 
for 
contribution 
from 
and 
participation 
by 
all 
donors 
and 
financial 
institutions that 
are 
providing 
or 
considering 
providing 
such 
budgetary 
support. 
5) 
The 
PF 
aims at  increasing 
the 
predictability 
of 
donor 
flows. 
This 
will 
benefit overall 
economic 
developments and 
the 
implementation 
and 
efficiency 
of 
programmes 
in 
priority 
sectors. 
The 
PF facility 
will 
provide 
general 
budget 
support 
co-ordinated among 
the 
participating 
donors and operated 
according 
to 
established 
government 
procedures and 
reporting 
requirements 
agreed 
with 
the 
partners. 
2. 
Justification 
6) 
Tanzania 
has 
implemented an 
economic 
reform 
programme 
that 
has 
shown 
a 
number 
of 
positive 
results. 
Significant 
progress 
has 
been 
made 
in 
macroeconomic stabilisation 
and 
structural 
reform 
of 
the 
22 
The 
Partnership 
Framework 
is 
the 
name 
given 
to 
the 
harmonised 
PRBS and 
PRSC 
budget 
support 
framework. 
23 
The 
term 
“Budget 
Support” 
as 
used 
to 
denote 
both 
budget 
and 
other 
similar 
support 
in 
the 
context 
of 
the 
PF 
Memorandum. 
110 
Page  121
Annex 
9 
economy. 
Inflation 
has 
stabilised 
below 
5 
percent 
and 
signs 
are 
that 
many 
of 
the 
elements 
required 
for 
a 
private 
sector 
led 
growth 
are 
in 
place. 
7) 
In 
the 
first 
years 
of 
the 
reform 
programme, 
growth 
rates 
in 
the 
economy 
were 
slow 
to 
pick 
up, 
however, and 
this 
led 
to 
lack 
of 
progress 
in 
poverty 
reduction 
and 
slow 
development 
of 
the 
economic 
base 
needed 
to 
support 
the 
public 
sector. 
Growth 
rates 
in 
the 
economy 
have 
improved 
in 
recent 
years, 
but 
poverty 
is 
still 
prevalent. 
It 
has 
been 
estimated 
that 
about one 
third 
of 
the 
population 
cannot 
afford 
basic 
needs 
for 
livelihood. 
As 
many 
as 
18 
percent 
are 
living 
in 
abject poverty, 
that 
is, 
in 
a 
situation 
where 
the 
income 
is 
insufficient 
to 
buy 
food 
to 
cover 
minimum 
nutritional 
needs. 
Tanzania 
needs 
several years 
of 
high 
per 
capita 
growth 
rates 
to 
reduce 
poverty 
levels 
significantly. 
8) 
Surveys 
of 
poverty 
and 
living 
conditions 
in 
Tanzania 
show 
that 
there 
are 
important 
challenges 
in 
the 
priority 
sectors 
that 
are 
detailed 
in 
the 
PRS.  These 
priority 
sectors 
(and 
priority 
items) 
may 
be 
subject 
to 
change 
during 
the 
ongoing 
process 
of 
reviewing 
and 
updating 
the 
PRS, 
but 
they 
currently 
include: 
health 
(primary); 
education 
(basic); 
water;  roads (rural); 
agriculture  (extension 
services); 
justice 
and 
HIV/ 
AIDS. 
There 
is 
furthermore 
a serious 
concern 
about 
the 
possibility 
of 
large 
segments 
of 
the 
population 
being 
left 
out 
of 
the economic 
growth 
and income generation 
process. 
In 
recognition 
of 
the 
enormous 
challenge 
in 
combating poverty 
the 
Government 
of 
the 
United 
Republic 
of 
Tanzania 
has 
refocused 
priorities 
towards  social 
sectors 
and 
primary 
services 
within 
them. 
Long-term 
targets 
for 
poverty  reduction 
were  articulated 
in 
the 
Vision 
2025  and  elaborated 
further 
in 
National 
Poverty 
Eradication 
Strategy 
(NPES) 
adopted 
by 
the 
Government 
in 
November 
1997. 
The 
Tanzania 
Assistance 
Strategy 
(TAS), 
which 
expresses 
the 
modalities 
and 
coordination 
requirements 
for 
donor 
support 
and 
the PRS focus 
on 
how 
to 
combat 
poverty 
in 
an 
effective 
manner, 
both 
in 
terms 
of 
more 
effective 
use 
of 
public 
resources 
and 
in 
improving 
the 
possibilities 
for 
economic  growth, 
employment 
creation 
and 
income 
generation. 
9) 
Domestic 
revenue 
to 
the 
public 
sector 
in 
Tanzania 
was 
12, 
2 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
the 
2001/02 budget 
year. 
The 
fiscal deficit 
before 
grants was 
6,6 
percent. 
In 
addition 
to 
the 
reforms 
carried 
out 
in 
tax 
policy 
and administration,  the Government 
recognizes 
that more 
measures are 
needed 
to 
enhance 
domestic 
resource 
mobilization. 
However, 
a 
realistic 
assessment 
is 
that 
the 
tax 
base, 
which 
is 
the 
monetized, 
non- 
governmental 
part 
of 
the 
economy, 
needs 
to 
grow 
faster 
than 
overall 
GDP 
for 
a 
number 
of 
years 
to 
yield 
a 
significantly 
higher 
tax/GDP 
ratio. 
While 
efforts 
to 
increase  revenue 
mobilisation 
are 
being 
implemented, 
the 
poverty 
reduction 
programme 
is 
in 
need 
of 
external 
grants 
or 
other  concessional 
financing. 
Direct 
budget 
support 
provides 
a  sustainable 
route 
to 
manage 
the  transition 
from 
aid 
dependency. 
It 
strengthens 
national 
ownership 
of 
the 
development 
process 
as 
well 
as 
Government 
institutions, 
and 
it 
facilitates 
the 
implementation 
of 
democratically 
selected sector strategies 
for 
growth 
and 
poverty 
reduction. 
111 
Page  122
Annex 
9 
3. 
The 
Performance 
Assessment 
Framework 
IO) 
The 
PF 
is 
linked 
to 
a 
common 
set 
of 
policy 
and 
poverty reduction 
goals. 
These 
goals are agreed 
between 
the 
Government 
of 
Tanzania and 
the 
participating 
donors and 
shall 
be 
based 
on 
the 
goals 
of 
the 
PRS. 
The 
goals, 
including 
the 
policies 
to 
reach 
them 
and 
the 
indicators 
to 
monitor 
them, 
are 
described 
in 
a 
Performance 
Assessment 
Framework 
(PAF)24. 
It 
is 
the 
intention 
of 
the 
parties 
to 
harmonize the PRS 
action 
plan 
and 
the 
PAF 
completely 
within 
three 
years. 
11) 
The 
PAF 
is 
a 
results-oriented 
matrix 
of 
agreed actions 
and  indicators 
including 
timeframe 
for 
reaching 
targets. 
The 
PAF 
sets 
out 
agreed 
key 
short and 
medium 
term 
actions 
to 
reduce 
poverty 
such 
as 
macroeconomic 
stability 
and 
performance  improvements 
in 
the 
public 
sector 
together 
with 
specific 
annual  measures 
to 
be 
taken 
by 
the 
Government 
to 
achieve 
the 
PRS 
targets. 
The 
indicators 
for 
the 
macroeconomic 
targets 
of 
the 
PAF 
will 
be 
those  elaborated 
in 
the 
IMF 
programme 
framework 
discussions. 
Dialogue 
on 
these 
issues 
with 
a 
wider 
groups 
of 
stakeholders 
will 
be 
handled 
in 
the 
appropriate 
common 
forum 
e.g. 
the 
PER 
macroeconomic 
group. 
The 
PF 
parties 
will 
avoid introducing 
new 
targets, 
indicators 
or 
measures 
that 
are 
outside 
the 
PAF 
and 
the 
IMF 
programme macroeconomic 
framework. 
12) 
The 
parties 
will 
use 
the 
PAF 
and 
PRS 
Review 
process 
as 
a 
tool 
for 
dialogue 
on 
PRS 
implementation 
and 
progress 
on 
PRS 
implementation 
and 
PAF 
actions 
will 
help 
to 
determine 
appropriate 
levels 
of 
future 
funding 
for 
the 
General 
Budget. 
Through 
a 
continuous 
process, 
it 
will 
allow 
the 
Government 
and 
the 
Donors 
to 
assess 
progress 
towards 
agreed 
medium 
term 
objectives 
and 
the 
implementation 
status 
of 
specific 
measures. 
The 
PAF 
will 
be 
updated 
annually 
as 
part 
of 
the 
Annual 
Review 
process. 
4. 
Review 
Process 
13) 
The 
review 
process 
for 
PF 
will 
be 
common 
for 
all 
participating 
donors and 
based 
on 
the 
review 
process 
for 
the 
PRS. 
The 
Government 
of 
Tanzania and 
the 
donors 
participating 
will 
maintain 
a close 
dialogue 
regarding the 
PF. 
The 
parties shall 
meet 
on 
an agreed schedule 
to 
review 
implementation 
and 
the 
attainment 
of 
objectives 
(see 
Appendix). 
The 
PAF 
matrix 
and PRS 
Progress 
Report 
will 
form 
the 
basis 
for 
discussions 
on 
progress 
between 
PF donors 
and 
the 
Government. 
14) 
The 
timing 
of 
review 
meetings 
through 
the 
year 
will 
be 
decided 
in 
such a 
way 
that the 
budget 
process 
is 
facilitated 
and that 
the 
PRSC requirements 
and 
the 
bilateral 
donors’ 
need 
for 
inputs 
to 
their 
decision-making 
processes 
are 
accommodated. 
The 
table 
in 
the 
Appendix 
lists 
the 
meetings 
and 
actions 
needed. 
15) 
Assessment 
of 
performance 
will 
be 
based 
on 
progress 
towards 
targets 
and 
measures 
identified 
through 
the 
PRS 
and 
expressed 
in 
the 
PAF 
matrix. 
Joint reviews 
of 
the 
PF 
Facility 
will 
be 
carried 
out 
by 
Government 
and 
all 
participating 
contributors. 
These 
reviews 
will 
use 
information 
gathered 
through 
ongoing 
processes 
to 
ensure 
that 
they 
do 
not 
set 
up 
a 
parallel 
review 
process. 
The 
review 
processes 
should 
endeavour 
to 
minimize 
additional technical 
work. 
However,  should 
the 
quality 
of 
regular 
information 
prove 
not 
to 
be satisfactory, 
the 
PF 
donors 
and 
the 
Government 
will 
agree 
on 
a 
timeframe 
and 
on 
the 
possible support 
donors 
could 
offer 
to 
build 
the 
capacity 
needed 
to 
provide 
this 
information. 
16) 
The 
review 
processes 
will 
draw 
together 
summary reports 
reviewing 
progress against 
relevant 
PAF 
and 
PRS 
targets. 
Progress against 
the 
list 
of 
indicators 
of 
social 
well-being, 
derived 
from 
the 
PRS 
and 
attached 
to 
the 
PAF 
matrix, should 
be 
covered 
in 
the 
context 
of 
the 
PRS 
and discussed 
as 
part 
of 
the 
24 
The 
PAF 
will 
form 
a 
part 
of 
the 
loan 
agreements 
under 
the 
PRSC 
programme 
with 
the 
World 
Bank 
and 
list 
the 
actions and 
prior 
actions under 
that 
programme.

APPENDIX 
Dates 
for 
review 
meetings 
will 
be 
determined 
by 
taking 
into 
account 
the 
preparation 
of 
the 
Tanzanian 
budget 
and 
by 
the 
presentation 
of 
the annual 
PRS 
report. 
The 
budget 
year 
is 
from 
1st 
of 
July 
to 
30th 
of 
June. 
The 
Annual Review 
process 
of 
updating 
the 
PAF 
targets 
will 
involve 
focused 
technical 
working 
groups 
for 
each 
area 
of 
the 
PAF. 
Each 
of 
these 
groups 
will 
comprise 
Government representatives, 
PRBS 
donor 
representatives 
and 
World 
Bank 
representatives. 
They 
will 
jointly 
review 
progress 
and 
priorities 
for 
the 
future. 
A 
final 
joint 
meeting 
involving 
all 
working 
groups 
will 
then 
be 
held to 
agree 
on 
key 
findings 
of 
the 
reviews 
and 
the 
way 
forward. 
The 
Mid-year 
Review 
will 
involve 
similar 
processes, 
but 
will 
be 
a 
light 
review 
of 
progress 
to 
date.  The 
Ministry 
of 
Finance, 
in 
close 
coordination 
with 
the 
Vice 
President's 
Office 
will 
maintain 
oversight 
of 
the 
Review 
process 
and 
coordinate inputs 
where necessary 
from 
other Government 
departments.

the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance, the 
Technical

will 
act 
as 
the 
main 
point 
of 
contact; 
they 
will 
co-ordinate 
preparation 
of 
the 
necessary 
Government 
assessments 
of 
progress

the 
information 
as 
outlined

17;

Annex 
10: 
Status 
of 
Public Procurement 
and Progress 
Made 
on 
the 
Procurement 
Reform 
in Mainland 
Tanzania. 
1. 
Procurement 
in 
Tanzania 
is 
in 
a 
reform 
stage 
based 
on 
the 
recommendations 
of 
the 
1996 
Country 
Procurement 
Assessment 
Report 
(CPAR). 
The 
Government 
enacted 
the Procurement 
Law 
in 
February 
2001 
replacing 
all 
previous procurement legislation. 
The 
Act 
became 
effective 
on 
July 
1, 
2001. 
In 
tandem, 
the  Government 
also 
issued 
the 
Regulations 
in 
two 
parts: 
(a) 
Procurement 
of 
Goods  and 
Works;  and 
(b) 
Selection and 
Employment 
of 
Consultants. 
The 
corresponding 
Regulations 
became 
effective 
on 
July 
2, 
2001. 
In 
mid 
November 
2002, 
the 
Government 
issued 
a 
set 
of 
Standard 
Bidding 
Documents 
(SBDs). 
These 
comprise: 
(a) 
Procurement 
of 
Goods 
through International 
Competitive 
Bidding; 
(b) 
Procurement 
of 
Health 
Sector 
Goods; 
(c) 
Standard 
Request 
for 
Proposal; 
(c) 
Standard 
Pre- 
qualification 
document 
for 
procurement 
of 
works; 
(d) 
Procurement 
of 
works  through 
National 
Competitive 
Bidding; 
and 
(e) 
Procurement 
of 
Works, 
Smaller 
contracts. 
These 
documents 
were 
reviewed 
during 
2003 
Country 
Procurement 
Assessment 
Report (CPAR) 
and 
appear 
to 
be 
in 
line 
with 
the 
Bank’s 
Standard 
Bidding 
Documents. 
2. 
The 
Public 
Procurement 
Act 
(PPA)  covers 
procurement 
in 
both 
central  government 
and 
local 
authorities, 
though 
the latter 
is 
covered 
by 
specific 
Local 
Government 
Regulations, 
to 
be 
issued 
under 
the 
Local 
Government 
Finance 
Act. 
The 
local 
government 
authority 
has 
finalized 
draft Regulations and 
Procurement 
Manual, 
which 
are 
in 
line 
with 
the 
new 
procurement 
law. 
Remarkable 
change 
in 
the 
new 
local 
government regulations 
is 
a change 
of 
composition 
of 
the 
local 
government 
authority 
tender 
board 
of 
which 
the 
councilors 
have 
been 
entirely 
removed 
from 
the 
procurement 
process. 
3. 
The 
Bank 
carried out 
another 
CPAR 
this 
year. 
The 
stakeholders

was

on

27 
- 
28, 
2003

Consultant.

alia; 
(a) 
To

Law

to

(c) 
Change 
legal

to

officers

4. 
This

again

The 
CPAR’s 
key 
findings, 
strengths

are

below. 
Procurement 
is 
a 
neglected 
function. 
Legal 
Framework: 
Overall 
the

fvamework 
is 
suficient

Key 
Findings, 
Strengths and 
Weaknesses 
Despite 
the 
economic 
benefits  mentioned 
above 
procurement 
has 
not 
to 
date 
been 
considered 
to 
be 
a 
core 
hnction 
of

worthy

The

for

in 
Tanzania 
is 
in 
transition. While 
some

important 
steps

particularly

to complaint 
and

Overall, 
and compared 
to  other  countries 
in 
the 
region, 
the 
legal 
framework 
for 
121 
Page  132
Annex 
10 
public 
procurement 
is 
updated 
and 
adequate. 
Since 
the 
introduction 
of 
the Procurement 
Act 
in 
200 
1, 
the 
prescribed Regulations 
for 
Local 
Government 
have 
been 
lacking. 
This 
has 
lead 
to

and

throughout 
in

It 
is 
essential, 
that 
the 
Local 
Government 
Procurement 
Regulations (now available 
in

be

as 
possible. 
At

have 
very 
limited

to 
a 
complaints 
mechanism. 
A

be

by 
strengthening 
the 
complaints

ability 
of 
the 
Regulatory 
Body 
and 
establishing the 
Public 
Appeals 
Authority, 
already entrenched 
in

provide a functioning 
avenue 
of

for 
bidders. 
Local 
Government Procurement 
needs 
further  attention 
Access 
to 
complaints should 
be 
strengthened

mu9t be

Current

Abusive

in

Publicity 
in public

Orpanization 
&

The

and 
hold 
only 
regulatory 
powers 
Procurement should 
be 
decentralized 
The single most 
important 
issue 
on 
the 
legal framework 
is 
the 
lack 
of 
dissemination 
and 
proper 
application 
of 
the 
rules. 
After  a period 
of 
carving 
out 
the 
new 
rules, 
the 
country 
must 
now 
focus 
on 
making 
those 
rules 
work. 
The 
knowledge 
of 
procurement 
rules 
and 
capacity 
to 
apply 
these 
rules 
is 
very poor 
in 
most 
of 
the 
procuring 
entities. 
Procurement audits 
are 
seldom 
performed 
and 
the 
consequences 
for 
breach 
of 
procurement 
rules, 
outside 
corruption 
cases, 
are 
minimal. 
As 
a 
consequence 
hereof, enforcement 
of 
the procurement 
rules 
suffers 
and 
malpractices 
in 
direct 
conflict 
with 
existing 
rules 
are 
allowed 
to 
flourish. 
Securing 
bidders 
and 
the 
public 
access 
to 
information 
is 
one 
of 
the 
cornerstones 
of 
sound 
procurement 
practices. 
Procuring 
entities 
are 
presently 
utilizing 
a 
number 
of 
different 
media 
for 
advertising 
procurement 
opportunities, 
making 
access 
to 
information 
and 
enforcement 
of 
the 
rules 
on 
advertisement 
difficult. 
A 
central 
media 
for  publication 
of 
procurement 
information, a 
Procurement 
Journal, 
should 
be 
established,  supplemented 
with 
an 
Internet information 
site. 
At 
present 
the 
Central 
Tender 
Board 
holds 
both 
executive 
and 
regulatory 
powers, 
which 
contributes 
to 
an 
unstable 
situation 
where 
the 
CTB 
on 
one 
hand 
participates 
in 
the 
procurement procedure 
and 
on 
the 
other 
hand 
monitors 
the 
same 
procedures. 
The 
executive 
role 
of 
the 
CTB 
also leads 
to 
lack 
of 
accountability 
and 
responsibility 
at 
the 
level 
of 
the 
ministerial 
tender  boards  and the 
ministerial Accounting  Officers, 
who 
are 
otherwise 
responsible 
for 
the 
budget 
but not for 
procurement under 
that body. 
As 
a 
result 
of 
the 
reform 
of 
the 
Central 
Tender 
Board, 
the 
executive functions 
of 
procurement 
should 
be 
decentralized 
to 
the 
level 
of 
ministries, 
departments, 
agencies 
and 
parastatals. 
This would 
make 
the 
accounting 
officers 
also 
responsible 
also 
for 
procurement. 
To 
support  the  decentralized 
procuring 
entities 
focus 
should 
turn 
to 
establishing/ 
strengthening procurement 
management 
units 
in 
these 
entities. 
When 
the 
CTB 
is 
reformed 
and 
becomes 
a purely regulatory authority 
it 
is 
important 
to 
ensure 
efficient information 
flow 
to 
enable 
the 
regulatory authority 
to 
monitor 
the 
entire 
procurement 
process 
on 
a 
continuous 
basis. 
Monitoring 
must 
be 
carried 
out 
at 
all 
stages 
of 
the  procurement 
process, 
from 
the 
early 
planning 
stages 
to 
contract 
management 
and 
implementation. 
The 
Government 
is 
a 
major procurer 
and 
as 
such 
must 
utilize 
the 
advantages 
in 
terms 
of 
bulk 
and 
low 
unit 
price 
procurement that 
is 
associated 
with  it. 
This 
can 
be  done 
by 
adopting 
the 
system 
of 
framework 
contracts, where 
the 
scale 
factor 
is 
used, 
whereas 
cost- 
consuming 
aspects such 
as 
storage 
and 
freight 
are 
avoided. 
At 
all 
levels 
of 
administration, 
procurement 
is 
being 
handled, 
often  improperly, by 
untrained, 
non-specialist 
staff, 
This 
constitutes 
a major 
weakness 
of 
the 
entire 
procurement 
system 
and 
it 
is 
important 
that 
measures 
for 
capacity 
building 
are 
devised 
as 
soon 
as 
possible 
and 
in 
a

A 
capacity 
building 
strategy 
for 
the 
entire 
public 
sector 
is 
required 
Informationflow 
must 
be 
ensured 
to 
allow 
for proper monitoring 
The advantage 
of 
scale 
must 
be 
better 
utilized 
Capacity

The lack

The public procurement 
environment 
is 
high-risk. 
Private 
Sector: 
The 
Costs 
offinancial

A

Tax

need

Zanzibar: 
Procurement 
system 
in 
need 
of 
a 
general 
overhaul 
rules. 
It 
is 
therefore 
important 
that 
such 
a 
cadre 
is 
established 
within 
the 
civil 
service 
system 
in 
replacement 
of 
the  current  supplies 
officer 
category. 
Likewise,  a 
new 
professional body 
for 
the 
procurement 
profession should 
be 
set 
up to 
replace the 
National 
Board 
of 
Materials 
Management. 
It 
is 
generally recognized that 
corruption 
is 
a 
widespread occurrence 
within 
procurement. 
Corruption 
in 
procurement 
is 
explained by 
a 
number 
of 
factors, 
including  lack 
of 
accountability 
of 
public 
servants 
and 
lack 
of 
transparency 
at 
certain 
stages 
of 
the 
procurement 
process. 
Low 
public 
service 
salaries, 
lack 
of 
morale 
- 
also 
on 
the 
part 
of 
the 
private 
sector, 
and 
greed were 
other 
cited 
reasons. 
It 
is 
estimated 
that 
at 
the 
national level 
about 
20 
percent 
of 
the 
government expenditure 
on procurement 
is 
lost 
through 
corruption 
In 
the 
recent 
years 
the 
National 
Audit 
Office 
has 
received 
increased 
funds 
and 
technical 
assistance. 
However, 
the 
office 
still 
needs 
additional input 
in 
order 
to carry 
out 
full 
and 
frequent procurement audits. 
This 
must 
be 
paired 
with 
measures 
to 
ensure 
better 
and more 
frequent 
internal 
control. 
At 
present there 
is 
very 
little 
involvement 
of 
the 
media 
and 
civil 
society 
in 
the 
procurement 
process. 
This 
is 
due 
to lack 
of 
capacity 
and 
lack 
of 
knowledge 
on 
how 
the 
civil 
society 
and 
the media may 
be 
employed 
in 
monitoring 
procurement 
at 
the 
various levels. 
There 
is 
a 
need 
to 
initiate 
capacity 
building 
measures 
enabling 
NGOs 
to 
monitor 
procurement 
processes 
- 
especially 
at 
the 
local 
government 
level, 
and 
to 
introduce 
and 
inform 
about 
available 
whistleblowing 
mechanisms. 
Based 
on 
the  analysis 
of 
its  legislative 
framework, the 
effectiveness 
of 
its 
regulatory 
institutions, 
the 
strength 
of 
its 
enforcement regime, 
the 
capacity 
of 
its institutional 
and 
human 
resources, 
and the 
threat 
of 
corruption, 
the 
assessment 
found 
that 
the 
environment 
for 
conducting 
public 
procurement 
in 
Tanzania 
is 
high-risk. 
Tanzania’s 
effort 
to 
reform 
and 
improve 
its 
trade 
environment 
has 
picked 
up 
speed 
over 
the 
last 
five 
years. 
The most 
important 
factors 
for 
this improvement 
are 
more 
transparent 
and 
lower 
tariff 
rates 
and 
an 
elimination 
of 
most 
export 
and 
import 
licenses 
since 
the 
mid 
1980s. 
Obtaining 
bid 
security 
and 
letters 
of 
credit 
is 
very costly. 
The 
value 
of 
the 
security must 
either 
be 
100% 
cash 
cover or 
the value 
of 
the 
contract 
must 
be 
at 
least 
125% 
of 
the 
secured 
amount. The 
high lending 
rates and 
costs 
of 
bid 
security 
are caused 
by 
the 
unstable 
legal 
environment, the 
high 
cost 
of 
capital, 
a high 
degree 
of 
risk, 
general 
loan 
and 
collateral 
recovery problems. 
Commercial 
lending 
rates 
are 
high 
(I 
6.96% 
in 
November 
2002). 
Tanzania 
has 
pre-shipment 
inspection 
(PSI) 
and 
this 
is 
according to generally 
established 
procedures. 
However, 
there 
is 
strong 
evidence 
that 
PSI, 
due 
to  external 
factors, 
i.e. 
development 
of 
technology, 
is 
inefficient, 
and 
the 
introduction 
of 
a 
Post 
Entry 
Audit 
Program 
in 
December 
1999 
to 
inspect 
the 
already 
inspected 
goods 
confirms this notion. 
All 
indicators 
point 
towards 
an 
urgent 
need 
to 
move 
from 
PSI 
to 
Destination 
Inspection. 
Companies 
generally complain that 
they cannot 
obtain 
proper 
closure 
of 
their 
tax payments 
at 
the 
end 
of 
the 
year. 
The 
common pattern 
is 
that the Tanzanian 
Revenue 
Authority 
after 
4-5 
years 
will 
contact 
the company and 
demand 
to 
review 
all 
books, accounts, 
receipts, 
etc. 
This practice 
leads 
to 
an 
unstable 
environment 
where 
the 
cost 
of 
conducting 
business 
is 
difficult 
to calculate. 
The 
procurement 
system 
in 
Zanzibar 
is 
flawed 
and 
does 
not function properly 
and 
there 
is 
a 
need 
for  a 
general 
overhaul. 
Reforms should 
include  a 
new 
legal 
framework 
and 
decentralisation 
of 
procurement 
to 
ministerial 
and 
other 
levels. 
This 
must 
be 
associated 
with 
extensive capacity 
building 
and 
sensitisation 
at 
all 
levels. 
In 
addition,  efforts 
to 
establish 
an 
integrity 
and 
anti-corruption 
framework  must 
be 
stepped 
up, 
ideally 
in 
cooperation 
with 
institutions 
in 
Tanzania 
mainland.

Chapter 
1: 
Introduction 
This 
chapter 
introduces 
the context, 
the 
key 
objectives, 
and 
the 
rationale 
of 
the 
Analytical 
Report. 
The 
chapter 
will 
also 
describe 
the 
scope, 
organization 
of 
the 
report, and 
any relevant 
reference 
that 
merit 
mentioning 
at 
the early 
stage. 
Chapter 
2: 
Methodology 
This 
chapter 
will 
describe 
the 
process and methods 
through 
which 
the 
report 
will 
be 
prepared. Since 
the 
report 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
a 
result 
of 
the 
series 
of 
consultations and 
participation 
of 
various 
stakeholders, 
it 
is 
necessary 
that 
this 
process 
be 
presented 
and 
known 
to 
the 
users 
of 
the 
report. 
Various 
data 
sources 
and 
studies, 
both 
already 
available and 
those 
to 
be 
commissioned 
on specific 
areas 
or 
available 
from 
independent 
studies 
would 
be 
described 
in 
this 
chapter. 
Chapter 
3: 
The 
Status 
of 
Poverty 
This 
chapter 
will 
present 
the 
status 
of 
poverty 
as 
indicated 
by 
a 
combination 
of 
indicators 
of 
human 
development. 
In 
addition 
to 
the 
status 
of 
poverty, 
the  annual 
analytical report 
will 
also 
examine 
the 
trends 
in 
poverty 
based 
on 
the 
results 
of 
major 
surveys. 
Main 
areas 
of 
focus 
will 
be: 
0 
A 
brief 
analytical 
presentation 
of 
the 
trend 
and 
magnitudes 
of 
various income 
poverty 
indicators 
(e.g. 
consumption, 
assets 
ownership, 
employment, 
dependency  ratios, 
access 
to 
water  and 
sanitation, 
etc.) 
0 
Standard 
of 
living, 
as 
measured 
by 
real 
GDP 
per 
capita 
expressed 
in 
PPP, 
which 
indicates the 
level 
of 
people’s 
choice 
to 
have 
access 
to 
the  resources 
needed 
to 
for 
a decent standard 
of 
living. 
0 
Longevity, 
as 
measured 
by 
life 
expectancy at 
birth, 
which 
gives 
an 
indication 
of 
the 
level 
of 
one 
essential 
human 
choice 
of 
living 
a 
long 
and 
health 
life. 
Since 
the 
trend 
in 
the 
key 
health 
parameters 
is 
essential 
in 
explaining 
the 
observed 
trend 
in 
life 
expectancy, 
it

necessary

the 
trend 
in

infant mortality, main

death, 
population 
per 
health facilities, and

(e.g. 
Aids

0 
Educational 
attainment, 
as 
measured 
by 
a 
combination 
of 
adult 
literacy 
and 
enrolment 
ratios, 
which 
indicates 
the 
level 
of 
human 
choice 
to 
acquire 
knowledge. 
0 
Human 
poverty 
indices, 
measuring 
the

deprivation:

food

0 
Qualitative 
aspects 
of

and 
how 
the 
how 
human 
choices have been 
impacted: 
political 
freedom,

processes, 
social 
capital, security, etc.

Chapter 
4: 
Poverty 
Location, Diversity, and 
Vulnerability 
This 
chapter 
presents 
some 
key 
features 
of 
poverty. 
More 
specifically 
it 
focuses 
on 
the 
location 
of 
poverty 
and 
its 
diversity. 
This 
discussion 
will 
be 
linked 
to 
vulnerability, 
because some 
of 
the 
features 
of 
poverty 
are 
determined 
by 
it. 
Factors 
that 
enhance 
vulnerability 
to 
poverty 
such 
as 
weather, 
civil 
strife, 
AIDS 
epidemic, etc. 
will 
be discussed 
here. 
These 
can 
be 
presented 
as 
follows: 
0 
Disaggregated 
human 
poverty 
indices, 
detailing location 
and 
diverse 
forms 
of 
poverty: 
e.g. 
urban 
poverty, 
rural 
poverty, 
gender 
poverty, 
regional 
poverty, 
etc. 
0 
Aggregate 
analysis 
of 
the 
location 
and 
diversity 
of 
vulnerability: 
Vulnerable 
groups, 
sectors, 
regions, 
age, 
gender, 
etc. 
and factors 
that 
make those groups/locations 
vulnerable. 
This 
analysis 
should 
cover 
both 
quantitative 
and 
qualitative 
(mainly 
from PPA 
results) 
parts. 
0 
Coping 
strategies at 
both 
household 
and 
community 
levels 
Chapter 
5: 
Macro 
and 
Micro 
Linkage 
to 
Poverty 
This 
chapter 
discusses 
the 
trend 
in 
economic 
growth 
in 
general, 
and 
later 
on 
looks 
at 
the 
structure 
of 
growth 
within 
the framework 
of 
implications 
on 
poverty reduction. 
A 
discussion 
is 
then 
made, 
based 
on 
the 
structural 
growth 
patterns 
established, 
on 
how 
policies 
and 
programs 
could 
be 
used 
to 
re-direct 
growth 
towards 
a 
more pro-poor 
direction. 
This 
analysis can be 
presented 
along 
the 
following 
lines: 
0 
Structural 
pattern and 
trend 
in 
economic growth. 
0 
Linkage 
of 
growth 
to 
poverty reduction (tracing 
whether 
growth 
is 
pro-poor) 
0 
Policy 
intervention 
needed 
to 
enhance 
pro-poor 
growth, 
both 
in 
terms 
of 
re-directing 
the 
current 
growing 
sectors 
to 
being 
pro-poor and stimulating 
growth 
in 
pro-poor 
sectors. 
Chapter 
6: 
Prospects 
and 
Challenges 
of 
Poverty 
Reduction 
in 
Tanzania 
This 
chapter 
seeks 
to 
provoke 
discussions 
on 
forward-looking 
issues 
revolving 
around 
prospects 
and 
challenges 
of 
reducing poverty. 
It 
is 
important 
to 
articulate 
these 
issues 
in 
this 
report 
because 
strategic 
consideration 
of 
such 
issues 
in 
updating 
the 
PRSP 
could 
be 
necessary. 
This 
can be presented 
as 
follows: 
0 
Prospects 
for 
poverty 
reduction: 
Resources 
available 
and 
the 
character 
of 
allocation 
and 
prioritization 
- 
Good 
governance 
i.e. 
Governance  based 
on 
will 
and 
commitment,  and 
capable 
of 
carrying out 
sound 
macroeconomic 
management, 
empowering 
and 
enabling 
people 
to 
participate 
in 
the 
development 
process. 
- 
Development 
partnership 
i.e. 
a 
reliable unilateral 
and 
multilateral 
donor 
support 
that 
will 
facilitate 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
PRSP. 
0 
Challenges 
of 
poverty 
reduction efforts 
in 
Tanzania. 
Poverty  reduction  and 
human  development 
challenges 
will 
be 
discussed 
along 
each 
key 
challenge, 
focusing 
on how 
each 
of 
them 
relates 
to 
poverty, 
what 
challenges 
they 
pose 
to 
poverty 
reduction initiatives, 
and 
how 
successful 
policy 
interventions 
could 
be incorporated 
in 
the 
process 
of 
implementing 
the 
PRSP: 
125 
Page  136
Annex 
11 
- 
HIV/AIDS 
epidemic- 
impact 
of 
this 
epidemic 
to 
the 
productive 
capacity 
of 
the 
economy, 
health 
expenditures 
on 
HIV 
related 
diseases 
rather than 
on 
enhancing 
human 
capability 
and 
nutritional 
health, 
etc. 
- 
Drug 
abuse 
and 
trafficking 
- 
impact 
on 
the 
productive labor 
force, 
distortions 
in 
the 
commodity, 
factor, and 
capital 
markets, etc. 
- 
Child 
labor- 
consequences 
on 
the 
future 
productive 
capacity 
of 
the 
population, 
poverty 
circle, 
costs 
and benefits 
of 
eliminating 
child 
labor, 
etc. 
- 
Refugee crisis- the 
extent 
to 
which 
the 
influx 
of 
refugees 
crowd 
out 
resources 
available 
for 
human development 
through 
increased 
surveillance and 
policing 
costs, 
etc. 
- 
Globalization 
- 
the 
effects 
of 
an increased 
degree 
in 
integration 
of 
economies 
and 
porous 
national 
boundaries 
on 
employment, 
income 
generation, 
and consumption, 
etc. 
- 
Culture 
- 
the  effect 
of 
attitudes,  norms, 
and  social 
behavior 
among 
the 
population 
inclined 
towards 
communal 
values and over-dependency 
on 
the 
government 
to 
provide 
both 
social and 
private 
goods, 
etc. 
- 
Policy 
trade-offs 
-such 
as 
those 
between 
public 
provision 
of 
infrastructure and 
enabling 
environment 
to 
communities 
one 
hand, 
and 
direct 
transfers 
on 
the 
other. 
- 
Environment 
- 
the 
extent 
to which 
both 
national 
and 
global 
perspectives 
and 
priorities 
on 
environment 
account 
for 
major 
development trade-offs, etc. 
- 
Sustainable 
Development 
- 
whether the 
current 
generation 
is 
earning 
its 
living 
without 
compromising 
the 
ability 
of 
future 
generation 
to 
earn 
its 
living, or 
whether 
the 
current 
economic 
growth 
affect 
environmental 
or 
human 
capital 
that 
will 
impair 
future 
economic 
growth?

Relation 
with 
the
